{"parentUuid":"62bb1cd2-8684-430d-813c-e5a74a02b0b0","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"progress","data":{"type":"hook_progress","hookEvent":"PostToolUse","hookName":"PostToolUse:Read","command":"callback"},"parentToolUseID":"toolu_01PRbxYygECqHYQEVPeHhiMb","toolUseID":"toolu_01PRbxYygECqHYQEVPeHhiMb","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:54.500Z","uuid":"d80afcd8-1914-480d-94c1-3b31d3a859bc","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}
{"parentUuid":"62bb1cd2-8684-430d-813c-e5a74a02b0b0","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01PRbxYygECqHYQEVPeHhiMb","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations.Basic\n     2→import Catlib.Foundations.Axioms\n     3→\n     4→/-!\n     5→# The Mathematics of Love: A Typed Love Relation\n     6→\n     7→## The problem\n     8→\n     9→The Catechism uses \"love\" in at least four different senses without always\n    10→distinguishing them. Axioms.lean declares:\n    11→\n    12→- `godIsLove : Prop` — God's nature is love\n    13→- `loves : Person → Person → Prop` — an untyped love relation\n    14→- `loveRequiresFreedom : Prop` — genuine love requires freedom\n    15→\n    16→But \"love\" in §1 John 4:8 (God IS love), §1827 (charity increases through\n    17→virtue), Mt 5:44 (love your enemies), and CCC §2264 (self-love grounds\n    18→legitimate defense) are NOT the same concept. Using a single `loves` predicate\n    19→collapses distinctions the Catechism itself makes.\n    20→\n    21→A proof assistant forces the disambiguation.\n    22→\n    23→## The four kinds\n    24→\n    25→Following the classical tradition (with Catechism support):\n    26→\n    27→1. **Agape** (charity): willing the good of the other for their own sake.\n    28→   The supernatural love that CCC §1827 says increases through virtue and\n    29→   CCC §1855 says mortal sin destroys. This is the love of Mt 5:44 (\"love\n    30→   your enemies\") — it does NOT require reciprocity.\n    31→\n    32→2. **Eros** (desire-love): attraction toward the good, the beautiful, the\n    33→   true. Benedict XVI (Deus Caritas Est §3-8) argues this is NOT opposed to\n    34→   agape but is its natural starting point. Eros is involuntary — you do not\n    35→   choose whom you find beautiful.\n    36→\n    37→3. **Philia** (friendship): mutual benevolence between persons. Aquinas\n    38→   (ST II-II q.23 a.1) defines charity AS a friendship with God — but\n    39→   friendship requires mutuality by definition.\n    40→\n    41→4. **Self-love**: legitimate love of self. CCC §2264 (\"Love toward oneself\n    42→   remains a fundamental principle of morality\") — this grounds legitimate\n    43→   defense.\n    44→\n    45→## Findings\n    46→\n    47→- **Agape requires freedom; eros does not.** The axiom `loveRequiresFreedom`\n    48→  is over-general. You can be involuntarily attracted (eros) but you cannot\n    49→  be forced to will someone's good (agape). This matters for the hell\n    50→  doctrine: the damned may still experience eros (desire for goods they\n    51→  lack) but have definitively refused agape toward God.\n    52→\n    53→- **Mortal sin destroys agape specifically.** CCC §1855 says mortal sin\n    54→  \"destroys charity.\" But sinners still feel attraction (eros) and maintain\n    55→  friendships (philia). The destruction is of the supernatural love-kind,\n    56→  not all love. This resolves a pastoral puzzle: how can mortal sinners\n    57→  still love their families?\n    58→\n    59→- **Philia is symmetric; agape is not.** God loves (agape) the damned\n    60→  (S2: universal salvific will), but the damned do not love God back.\n    61→  Agape is not symmetric. Philia, by definition, requires mutuality.\n    62→\n    63→- **Self-love is reflexive by definition.** The lover and beloved are the\n    64→  same person. This is not a theorem — it is a structural property of the\n    65→  type.\n    66→\n    67→- **The ordo amoris is a strict ordering.** Aquinas (ST II-II q.26):\n    68→  love of God > love of neighbor > love of self. This ordering applies\n    69→  to agape specifically.\n    70→\n    71→## Denominational scope\n    72→\n    73→The four-fold distinction is broadly ecumenical — C.S. Lewis's \"The Four\n    74→Loves\" is a Protestant articulation of essentially the same taxonomy. The\n    75→ordo amoris is specifically Thomistic but broadly shared in substance.\n    76→\n    77→## Open questions\n    78→\n    79→Documented inline below — these are genuine gaps, not formalization failures.\n    80→-/\n    81→\n    82→set_option autoImplicit false\n    83→\n    84→namespace Catlib.Foundations.Love\n    85→\n    86→open Catlib\n    87→\n    88→-- ============================================================================\n    89→-- ## Core Types\n    90→-- ============================================================================\n    91→\n    92→/-- The four kinds of love the Catechism distinguishes (often implicitly).\n    93→\n    94→    This taxonomy follows the classical tradition. The Catechism does not\n    95→    use these Greek terms, but its text operates with exactly these\n    96→    distinctions: charity (agape) is supernatural and can be lost;\n    97→    attraction (eros) is involuntary; friendship (philia) requires\n    98→    mutuality; self-love (CCC §2264) grounds legitimate defense.\n    99→\n   100→    Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL — C.S. Lewis, Benedict XVI, Aquinas,\n   101→    and most Christian traditions recognize these distinctions. -/\n   102→inductive LoveKind where\n   103→  /-- Charity: willing the good of the other for their own sake.\n   104→      CCC §1822: \"Charity is the theological virtue by which we love God\n   105→      above all things for his own sake, and our neighbor as ourselves\n   106→      for the love of God.\" -/\n   107→  | agape\n   108→  /-- Desire-love: attraction toward the good/beautiful.\n   109→      Benedict XVI, Deus Caritas Est §3-8.\n   110→      Involuntary — you do not choose your attractions. -/\n   111→  | eros\n   112→  /-- Friendship: mutual benevolence between persons.\n   113→      Aquinas ST II-II q.23 a.1: charity is a friendship with God. -/\n   114→  | philia\n   115→  /-- Legitimate love of self.\n   116→      CCC §2264: \"Love toward oneself remains a fundamental principle\n   117→      of morality. It is therefore legitimate to insist on respect for\n   118→      one's own right to life.\" -/\n   119→  | selfLove\n   120→\n   121→/-- A typed love relation — specifying WHICH kind of love.\n   122→\n   123→    This replaces the untyped `loves : Person → Person → Prop` from\n   124→    Axioms.lean. The untyped version conflates four distinct concepts.\n   125→\n   126→    Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL (the taxonomy); the specific\n   127→    properties below vary. -/\n   128→structure TypedLove where\n   129→  /-- Which kind of love -/\n   130→  kind : LoveKind\n   131→  /-- The person who loves -/\n   132→  lover : Person\n   133→  /-- The person who is loved (for selfLove: lover = beloved) -/\n   134→  beloved : Person\n   135→  /-- Love intensity/degree (charity can increase per CCC §1827).\n   136→      0 = absent, higher = more intense. -/\n   137→  degree : Nat\n   138→\n   139→-- ============================================================================\n   140→-- ## Axioms: Properties of the four kinds\n   141→-- ============================================================================\n   142→\n   143→/-!\n   144→### Property 1: Agape is NOT symmetric\n   145→\n   146→You can love (agape) enemies who hate you. Mt 5:44: \"Love your enemies\n   147→and pray for those who persecute you.\" God loves the damned (S2: universal\n   148→salvific will) but the damned do not love God back.\n   149→\n   150→Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL — all Christians accept Mt 5:44.\n   151→-/\n   152→\n   153→/-- Agape is not symmetric: there exist pairs where one loves the other\n   154→    (agape) but the reciprocal does not hold.\n   155→\n   156→    Source: [Scripture] Mt 5:44 (\"love your enemies\"); S2 (God desires\n   157→    all to be saved — including those who reject Him).\n   158→\n   159→    This is an existence claim: we assert that non-reciprocated agape\n   160→    is POSSIBLE, not that it always fails to be reciprocated. -/\n   161→axiom agape_not_symmetric_witness :\n   162→  ∃ (tl : TypedLove),\n   163→    tl.kind = LoveKind.agape ∧\n   164→    tl.degree > 0 ∧\n   165→    -- There is no reciprocal agape from beloved to lover\n   166→    ∀ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   167→      tl'.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   168→      tl'.lover = tl.beloved →\n   169→      tl'.beloved = tl.lover →\n   170→      tl'.degree = 0\n   171→\n   172→/-!\n   173→### Property 2: Philia IS symmetric\n   174→\n   175→Friendship requires mutuality by definition. If A is friends with B,\n   176→then B is friends with A. One-sided friendship is not friendship — it\n   177→is unrequited affection.\n   178→\n   179→Source: Aquinas ST II-II q.23 a.1; Aristotle, Nicomachean Ethics VIII.2.\n   180→Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL.\n   181→-/\n   182→\n   183→/-- Philia (friendship) is symmetric: if A has philia toward B, then B\n   184→    has philia toward A.\n   185→\n   186→    Source: [Philosophy] Aristotle NE VIII.2; Aquinas ST II-II q.23 a.1.\n   187→    \"Friendship... requires mutual benevolence.\" -/\n   188→axiom philia_symmetric :\n   189→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   190→    tl.kind = LoveKind.philia →\n   191→    ∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   192→      tl'.kind = LoveKind.philia ∧\n   193→      tl'.lover = tl.beloved ∧\n   194→      tl'.beloved = tl.lover ∧\n   195→      tl'.degree > 0\n   196→\n   197→/-!\n   198→### Property 3: Self-love is reflexive\n   199→\n   200→The lover and beloved are the same person by definition.\n   201→CCC §2264: \"Love toward oneself remains a fundamental principle of morality.\"\n   202→\n   203→Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL.\n   204→-/\n   205→\n   206→/-- Self-love is reflexive: lover = beloved.\n   207→\n   208→    Source: [Definition] CCC §2264.\n   209→    This is a constraint on well-formed TypedLove instances\n   210→    of the selfLove kind. -/\n   211→axiom self_love_reflexive :\n   212→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   213→    tl.kind = LoveKind.selfLove →\n   214→    tl.lover = tl.beloved\n   215→\n   216→/-!\n   217→### Property 4: Agape requires freedom; eros does NOT\n   218→\n   219→S1 declares `loveRequiresFreedom`, but this is over-general. You can\n   220→be involuntarily attracted (eros) to someone — attraction is not a\n   221→free act. But you CANNOT be forced to will someone's good for their\n   222→own sake (agape) — charity is a free act of the will.\n   223→\n   224→This distinction matters for:\n   225→- Hell: the damned may still experience eros (they desire goods they\n   226→  lack) but have definitively refused agape toward God.\n   227→- Concupiscence: fallen humans experience involuntary eros toward evil\n   228→  (disordered desire) which is not itself sinful (CCC §1264) precisely\n   229→  because it is not free.\n   230→\n   231→Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL (the distinction between voluntary\n   232→and involuntary love is broadly accepted).\n   233→-/\n   234→\n   235→/-- Agape (charity) requires freedom of the will.\n   236→\n   237→    Source: [Scripture] S1 (loveRequiresFreedom, restricted to agape);\n   238→    CCC §1822 (charity as a theological VIRTUE — virtues require free acts).\n   239→\n   240→    A person without free will cannot perform genuine charity. -/\n   241→axiom agape_requires_freedom :\n   242→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   243→    tl.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   244→    tl.degree > 0 →\n   245→    tl.lover.hasFreeWill = true\n   246→\n   247→/-- Eros does NOT require freedom. Involuntary attraction is possible\n   248→    and is not sinful precisely because it is not free.\n   249→\n   250→    Source: CCC §1264 (concupiscence — involuntary disordered desire —\n   251→    is not itself sinful); Benedict XVI, Deus Caritas Est §3-8.\n   252→\n   253→    Formally: there can exist eros with degree > 0 where the lover\n   254→    lacks free will (or equivalently, eros can occur involuntarily). -/\n   255→axiom eros_does_not_require_freedom :\n   256→  ∃ (tl : TypedLove),\n   257→    tl.kind = LoveKind.eros ∧\n   258→    tl.degree > 0 ∧\n   259→    tl.lover.hasFreeWill = false\n   260→\n   261→/-!\n   262→### Property 5: Charity is graded — it can increase AND be destroyed\n   263→\n   264→CCC §1827: \"The practice of all the virtues is animated and inspired by\n   265→charity... Charity upholds and purifies our human ability to love, and\n   266→raises it to the supernatural perfection of divine love.\"\n   267→\n   268→CCC §1855: \"Mortal sin destroys charity in the heart of man.\"\n   269→\n   270→So charity (agape) has a degree that can increase through virtue and\n   271→drop to zero through mortal sin.\n   272→\n   273→Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL in substance (all Christians agree\n   274→charity can grow); the claim that mortal sin DESTROYS it is Catholic\n   275→(Protestants may frame the loss differently).\n   276→-/\n   277→\n   278→/-- Charity can increase: virtuous acts increase the degree of agape.\n   279→\n   280→    Source: [Definition] CCC §1827.\n   281→    Formalized as: for any agape love, there exists a greater degree. -/\n   282→axiom charity_can_increase :\n   283→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   284→    tl.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   285→    ∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   286→      tl'.kind = LoveKind.agape ∧\n   287→      tl'.lover = tl.lover ∧\n   288→      tl'.beloved = tl.beloved ∧\n   289→      tl'.degree > tl.degree\n   290→\n   291→/-- Mortal sin can reduce charity to zero.\n   292→\n   293→    Source: [Definition] CCC §1855 (\"Mortal sin destroys charity\").\n   294→    Formalized as: grave sin can produce a state where agape degree = 0. -/\n   295→axiom mortal_sin_can_destroy_charity :\n   296→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove) (s : Sin),\n   297→    tl.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   298→    isGraveSin s →\n   299→    ∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   300→      tl'.kind = LoveKind.agape ∧\n   301→      tl'.lover = tl.lover ∧\n   302→      tl'.beloved = tl.beloved ∧\n   303→      tl'.degree = 0\n   304→\n   305→/-!\n   306→### Property 6: Mortal sin destroys charity SPECIFICALLY\n   307→\n   308→CCC §1855: \"Mortal sin destroys charity in the heart of man.\" But it\n   309→does NOT destroy eros or philia. A person in mortal sin can still:\n   310→- Feel attraction (eros) to beauty, goodness, truth\n   311→- Maintain genuine friendships (philia)\n   312→- Love themselves (self-love)\n   313→\n   314→Only the supernatural love — willing the other's good FOR GOD'S SAKE —\n   315→is destroyed. This resolves the pastoral puzzle: how can mortal sinners\n   316→still love their families? They love with eros and philia, not agape.\n   317→\n   318→Denominational scope: CATHOLIC — this is the Catholic understanding of\n   319→mortal sin's effects. Protestants who reject the mortal/venial distinction\n   320→would frame this differently.\n   321→-/\n   322→\n   323→/-- Mortal sin does NOT destroy eros.\n   324→\n   325→    Source: Implied by CCC §1855 (which says charity specifically) and\n   326→    CCC §1264 (concupiscence — involuntary desire — persists even in grace).\n   327→\n   328→    A person in mortal sin retains the capacity for attraction. -/\n   329→axiom mortal_sin_preserves_eros :\n   330→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove) (s : Sin),\n   331→    tl.kind = LoveKind.eros →\n   332→    tl.degree > 0 →\n   333→    isGraveSin s →\n   334→    -- Eros is not affected: the love remains\n   335→    ∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   336→      tl'.kind = LoveKind.eros ∧\n   337→      tl'.lover = tl.lover ∧\n   338→      tl'.beloved = tl.beloved ∧\n   339→      tl'.degree > 0\n   340→\n   341→/-- Mortal sin does NOT destroy philia.\n   342→\n   343→    Source: Implied by CCC §1855 (charity specifically destroyed).\n   344→    A person in mortal sin retains natural friendships. -/\n   345→axiom mortal_sin_preserves_philia :\n   346→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove) (s : Sin),\n   347→    tl.kind = LoveKind.philia →\n   348→    tl.degree > 0 →\n   349→    isGraveSin s →\n   350→    ∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   351→      tl'.kind = LoveKind.philia ∧\n   352→      tl'.lover = tl.lover ∧\n   353→      tl'.beloved = tl.beloved ∧\n   354→      tl'.degree > 0\n   355→\n   356→/-!\n   357→### Property 7: The Ordo Amoris — a strict ordering on love\n   358→\n   359→Aquinas, ST II-II q.26: there is a proper ORDER to love. We should love:\n   360→1. God above all\n   361→2. Neighbor as ourselves\n   362→3. Ourselves (legitimately)\n   363→\n   364→This applies to agape specifically. The ordering is:\n   365→  love of God > love of neighbor > love of self\n   366→\n   367→We model this as a strict ordering on targets of agape.\n   368→\n   369→Denominational scope: BROADLY SHARED — all Christians accept the\n   370→Great Commandment (Mt 22:37-39) which establishes this ordering.\n   371→The Thomistic formalization is Catholic; the substance is ecumenical.\n   372→-/\n   373→\n   374→/-- The targets of the ordo amoris. -/\n   375→inductive LoveTarget where\n   376→  /-- God — the supreme object of love -/\n   377→  | god\n   378→  /-- Neighbor — other persons -/\n   379→  | neighbor\n   380→  /-- Self — legitimate self-love -/\n   381→  | self_\n   382→\n   383→/-- The ordo amoris: a strict ordering on love targets.\n   384→\n   385→    Source: [Scripture] Mt 22:37-39 (Great Commandment);\n   386→    [Philosophy] Aquinas ST II-II q.26.\n   387→\n   388→    Love of God > love of neighbor > love of self.\n   389→    This means: when love-targets conflict, the higher-ranked\n   390→    target takes priority. -/\n   391→axiom ordo_amoris_god_over_neighbor :\n   392→  ∀ (p : Person) (tl_god tl_neighbor : TypedLove),\n   393→    tl_god.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   394→    tl_neighbor.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   395→    tl_god.lover = p →\n   396→    tl_neighbor.lover = p →\n   397→    -- God should be loved more than neighbor\n   398→    tl_god.degree > tl_neighbor.degree →\n   399→    True  -- This ordering is the NORM, not always the actuality\n   400→\n   401→axiom ordo_amoris_neighbor_over_self :\n   402→  ∀ (p : Person) (tl_neighbor tl_self : TypedLove),\n   403→    tl_neighbor.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   404→    tl_self.kind = LoveKind.selfLove →\n   405→    tl_neighbor.lover = p →\n   406→    tl_self.lover = p →\n   407→    -- Neighbor should be loved more than self\n   408→    tl_neighbor.degree > tl_self.degree →\n   409→    True\n   410→\n   411→/-- Denominational tag for the ordo amoris. -/\n   412→def ordo_amoris_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   413→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.ecumenical],\n   414→    note := \"Mt 22:37-39; Aquinas ST II-II q.26; broadly shared\" }\n   415→\n   416→-- ============================================================================\n   417→-- ## Theorems\n   418→-- ============================================================================\n   419→\n   420→/-!\n   421→### Theorem 1: Self-love grounds legitimate defense\n   422→\n   423→CCC §2264: \"Love toward oneself remains a fundamental principle of\n   424→morality. It is therefore legitimate to insist on respect for one's\n   425→own right to life.\"\n   426→\n   427→The derivation: self-love (a recognized form of love) implies that\n   428→one's own life is a good to be preserved. Therefore defending one's\n   429→life against unjust aggression is a legitimate expression of self-love.\n   430→\n   431→This connects Love.lean to LegitimateDefense.lean.\n   432→-/\n   433→\n   434→/-- Self-love provides the moral basis for legitimate defense.\n   435→\n   436→    Derivation chain:\n   437→    1. Self-love is a legitimate form of love (LoveKind.selfLove exists)\n   438→    2. Self-love is reflexive (self_love_reflexive)\n   439→    3. Love affirms the good of the beloved (definition of love)\n   440→    4. Therefore self-love affirms one's own good, including one's life\n   441→    5. Therefore defending one's life is a legitimate expression of self-love\n   442→\n   443→    Source: CCC §2264 makes this derivation explicitly. -/\n   444→theorem self_love_grounds_self_defense :\n   445→  ∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   446→    tl.kind = LoveKind.selfLove →\n   447→    tl.degree > 0 →\n   448→    -- Self-love implies lover = beloved (reflexivity)\n   449→    tl.lover = tl.beloved := by\n   450→  intro tl hkind _hdeg\n   451→  exact self_love_reflexive tl hkind\n   452→\n   453→/-!\n   454→### Theorem 2: Mortal sin destroys charity but not eros\n   455→\n   456→This is the key theorem explaining why sinners can still love.\n   457→CCC §1855 says mortal sin \"destroys charity\" — and ONLY charity.\n   458→\n   459→After mortal sin:\n   460→- Agape (charity): degree can reach 0\n   461→- Eros (attraction): preserved — sinners still find things beautiful\n   462→- Philia (friendship): preserved — sinners still have friends\n   463→- Self-love: preserved — sinners still care about themselves\n   464→-/\n   465→\n   466→/-- Mortal sin destroys agape but leaves eros intact.\n   467→\n   468→    This explains how someone in mortal sin can still love their\n   469→    family: they love with eros and philia, not with supernatural\n   470→    charity (agape). The supernatural dimension is destroyed; the\n   471→    natural dimensions remain.\n   472→\n   473→    Source: CCC §1855 (\"Mortal sin destroys charity in the heart of\n   474→    man\") — charity specifically, not love in general. -/\n   475→theorem mortal_sin_destroys_charity_not_eros\n   476→  (tl_agape : TypedLove) (tl_eros : TypedLove) (s : Sin)\n   477→  (h_agape_kind : tl_agape.kind = LoveKind.agape)\n   478→  (h_eros_kind : tl_eros.kind = LoveKind.eros)\n   479→  (h_eros_pos : tl_eros.degree > 0)\n   480→  (h_grave : isGraveSin s) :\n   481→  -- Agape CAN be destroyed...\n   482→  (∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   483→    tl'.kind = LoveKind.agape ∧\n   484→    tl'.lover = tl_agape.lover ∧\n   485→    tl'.beloved = tl_agape.beloved ∧\n   486→    tl'.degree = 0) ∧\n   487→  -- ...but eros SURVIVES\n   488→  (∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   489→    tl'.kind = LoveKind.eros ∧\n   490→    tl'.lover = tl_eros.lover ∧\n   491→    tl'.beloved = tl_eros.beloved ∧\n   492→    tl'.degree > 0) := by\n   493→  constructor\n   494→  · exact mortal_sin_can_destroy_charity tl_agape s h_agape_kind h_grave\n   495→  · exact mortal_sin_preserves_eros tl_eros s h_eros_kind h_eros_pos h_grave\n   496→\n   497→/-!\n   498→### Theorem 3: Agape is not symmetric (constructive)\n   499→\n   500→God loves (agape) all persons (S2: universal salvific will). But not\n   501→all persons love God back. Therefore agape is not symmetric.\n   502→\n   503→This is stronger than the witness axiom: it derives non-symmetry from\n   504→the universal salvific will combined with the existence of the damned\n   505→(who, by definition, have refused communion with God).\n   506→-/\n   507→\n   508→/-- Agape is not symmetric: one can love (agape) someone who does not\n   509→    reciprocate. God loves (agape) the damned, but the damned do not\n   510→    love God.\n   511→\n   512→    This follows from:\n   513→    - S2 (universal salvific will: God desires all to be saved)\n   514→    - The damned exist (they refused God — from Hell.lean)\n   515→    - Therefore: God loves them; they do not love God back\n   516→\n   517→    Denominational scope: ECUMENICAL — all Christians accept Mt 5:44. -/\n   518→theorem agape_not_symmetric :\n   519→  -- If there exists a person who is loved (agape) by another but\n   520→  -- does not reciprocate, then agape is not symmetric.\n   521→  (∃ (a b : Person) (d₁ : Nat),\n   522→    d₁ > 0 ∧\n   523→    ∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   524→      tl.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   525→      tl.lover = b →\n   526→      tl.beloved = a →\n   527→      tl.degree = 0) →\n   528→  -- Then there is no guarantee of symmetry\n   529→  ¬(∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   530→      tl.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   531→      tl.degree > 0 →\n   532→      ∃ (tl' : TypedLove),\n   533→        tl'.kind = LoveKind.agape ∧\n   534→        tl'.lover = tl.beloved ∧\n   535→        tl'.beloved = tl.lover ∧\n   536→        tl'.degree > 0) := by\n   537→  intro ⟨a, b, d₁, hd₁, hno_recip⟩ hsym\n   538→  -- Construct a love instance from a to b with degree d₁\n   539→  let tl_ab : TypedLove := {\n   540→    kind := LoveKind.agape\n   541→    lover := a\n   542→    beloved := b\n   543→    degree := d₁\n   544→  }\n   545→  -- By symmetry hypothesis, b should love a back\n   546→  have ⟨tl', hkind, hlover, hbeloved, hdeg⟩ := hsym tl_ab rfl hd₁\n   547→  -- But hno_recip says b→a agape has degree 0\n   548→  have := hno_recip tl' hkind hlover hbeloved\n   549→  -- degree = 0 contradicts degree > 0\n   550→  omega\n   551→\n   552→/-!\n   553→### Theorem 4: Love requires freedom ONLY for agape\n   554→\n   555→The axiom `loveRequiresFreedom` from Axioms.lean is over-general.\n   556→We show that agape requires freedom (agape_requires_freedom) while\n   557→eros does not (eros_does_not_require_freedom).\n   558→\n   559→This restricts the over-general axiom to its proper scope.\n   560→-/\n   561→\n   562→/-- Love-requires-freedom applies to agape but not eros.\n   563→\n   564→    Derivation:\n   565→    1. agape_requires_freedom: any agape with degree > 0 requires hasFreeWill\n   566→    2. eros_does_not_require_freedom: there exists eros with degree > 0\n   567→       where hasFreeWill = false\n   568→    3. Therefore \"love requires freedom\" is true for agape, false for eros\n   569→    4. The untyped axiom is an over-generalization\n   570→\n   571→    This is a STRUCTURAL finding: the untyped `loves` predicate in\n   572→    Axioms.lean cannot distinguish these cases. The typed version can. -/\n   573→theorem love_requires_freedom_only_agape :\n   574→  -- There exists love (eros) that does not require freedom\n   575→  (∃ (tl : TypedLove),\n   576→    tl.kind = LoveKind.eros ∧\n   577→    tl.degree > 0 ∧\n   578→    tl.lover.hasFreeWill = false) ∧\n   579→  -- But all agape requires freedom\n   580→  (∀ (tl : TypedLove),\n   581→    tl.kind = LoveKind.agape →\n   582→    tl.degree > 0 →\n   583→    tl.lover.hasFreeWill = true) := by\n   584→  constructor\n   585→  · exact eros_does_not_require_freedom\n   586→  · intro tl hkind hdeg\n   587→    exact agape_requires_freedom tl hkind hdeg\n   588→\n   589→-- ============================================================================\n   590→-- ## Open Questions\n   591→-- ============================================================================\n   592→\n   593→/-!\n   594→## OPEN QUESTION 1: Composition of love-kinds\n   595→\n   596→How do agape and eros combine in conjugal love? Benedict XVI (Deus Caritas\n   597→Est §3-8) argues that in married love, eros and agape are not simply summed\n   598→but TRANSFORMED — eros is \"purified\" and \"elevated\" by agape. But we have\n   599→no operator for this transformation.\n   600→\n   601→This is the SAME structural problem as diminisher composition in\n   602→culpability (see culpability-math article): the Catechism asserts that\n   603→multiple factors interact but does not specify the algebra.\n   604→\n   605→Possible models:\n   606→- **Sum**: conjugal_love = agape.degree + eros.degree\n   607→  Problem: Does not capture transformation.\n   608→- **Product**: conjugal_love = agape.degree * eros.degree\n   609→  Problem: If either is zero, total is zero. Is loveless attraction zero?\n   610→- **Transformation**: eros is INPUT to a function that produces elevated agape\n   611→  Problem: What is the function? Benedict XVI suggests it, but does not\n   612→  specify it.\n   613→\n   614→STATUS: OPEN. We cannot formalize conjugal love as a combination of\n   615→love-kinds without choosing an operator the magisterium has not specified.\n   616→-/\n   617→\n   618→/-!\n   619→## OPEN QUESTION 2: Can love reach zero across ALL dimensions?\n   620→\n   621→The damned have definitively refused agape toward God. But do they retain\n   622→self-love? They suffer — which implies they care about their own state,\n   623→which implies some form of self-love. Zero total love (all four kinds at\n   624→degree 0) may be impossible for a conscious being.\n   625→\n   626→If the damned retain self-love, then total love is never zero. This would\n   627→mean love is a strictly positive quantity for any existent conscious being —\n   628→a surprisingly strong claim.\n   629→\n   630→STATUS: OPEN. The Catechism does not address whether the damned retain\n   631→self-love. Aquinas (ST Supp. q.98) discusses the suffering of the damned\n   632→but does not resolve this question in terms we can formalize.\n   633→-/\n   634→\n   635→/-!\n   636→## OPEN QUESTION 3: \"God IS love\" as identity\n   637→\n   638→S1 declares `godIsLove : Prop` — God IS love. But this is modeled as a\n   639→PREDICATE (God has the property of being love), not an IDENTITY (God's\n   640→essence = love). The identity claim is much stronger: it says love is not\n   641→something God HAS but something God IS.\n   642→\n   643→In Lean, we could model this as:\n   644→  `axiom god_essence_is_love : God = Love`\n   645→But this requires `God` and `Love` to be the same TYPE, which our current\n   646→type system does not support (God is opaque, love is a relation).\n   647→\n   648→The identity claim connects to divine simplicity (God has no parts — all\n   649→God's attributes are identical with His essence). Fully capturing this\n   650→would require a much deeper metaphysical modeling framework.\n   651→\n   652→STATUS: OPEN. We model godIsLove as a predicate because Lean's type\n   653→system cannot directly express divine simplicity. This is a limitation\n   654→of the formalization, not of the theology.\n   655→-/\n   656→\n   657→/-!\n   658→## OPEN QUESTION 4: Is eros ordered toward agape?\n   659→\n   660→Benedict XVI (Deus Caritas Est §6-7) argues that eros is not simply a\n   661→different kind of love but is ORDERED TOWARD agape — eros naturally\n   662→tends toward transformation into agape when properly directed.\n   663→\n   664→If true, this would mean:\n   665→- Eros without agape is incomplete, not just different\n   666→- The proper trajectory of eros is toward agape\n   667→- Disordered eros is eros that resists transformation into agape\n   668→\n   669→This would introduce a TELEOLOGICAL ordering among love-kinds (not just\n   670→the ordo amoris among love-targets). But Benedict XVI suggests the\n   671→direction without specifying the mechanism.\n   672→\n   673→STATUS: OPEN. We can assert that eros \"should\" be ordered toward agape,\n   674→but we cannot formalize the transformation mechanism.\n   675→-/\n   676→\n   677→-- ============================================================================\n   678→-- ## Property Summary Table (for reference)\n   679→-- ============================================================================\n   680→\n   681→/-!\n   682→## Property Summary\n   683→\n   684→| Property                 | Agape | Eros  | Philia | Self-Love |\n   685→|--------------------------|-------|-------|--------|-----------|\n   686→| Reflexive                | No    | No    | No     | YES       |\n   687→| Symmetric                | NO    | No*   | YES    | N/A       |\n   688→| Transitive               | No    | No    | No     | N/A       |\n   689→| Ordered (ordo amoris)    | YES   | No    | No     | YES       |\n   690→| Requires freedom         | YES   | NO    | Yes**  | Yes**     |\n   691→| Destroyed by mortal sin  | YES   | NO    | NO     | NO        |\n   692→| Can increase             | YES   | Yes   | Yes    | Yes       |\n   693→| Supernatural             | YES   | No    | No     | No        |\n   694→\n   695→Notes:\n   696→* Eros can be unrequited (not symmetric), but this is different from\n   697→  agape's asymmetry — agape is NORMATIVELY asymmetric (love your enemies),\n   698→  while eros asymmetry is just factual (unrequited attraction).\n   699→** Philia and self-love require some degree of freedom for their full\n   700→   exercise, but the requirement is weaker than for agape.\n   701→-/\n   702→\n   703→-- ============================================================================\n   704→-- ## Denominational tags\n   705→-- ============================================================================\n   706→\n   707→/-- The four-fold love distinction: ecumenical. -/\n   708→def love_kinds_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   709→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.ecumenical],\n   710→    note := \"C.S. Lewis, Aquinas, Benedict XVI; broadly shared taxonomy\" }\n   711→\n   712→/-- Mortal sin destroys charity specifically: Catholic. -/\n   713→def mortal_sin_charity_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   714→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.catholic],\n   715→    note := \"CCC §1855; requires mortal/venial distinction (Catholic)\" }\n   716→\n   717→/-- Agape requires freedom: ecumenical. -/\n   718→def agape_freedom_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   719→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.ecumenical],\n   720→    note := \"Broadly shared; connects to S1 (love requires freedom)\" }\n   721→\n   722→/-- Self-love grounds legitimate defense: ecumenical. -/\n   723→def self_love_defense_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   724→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.ecumenical],\n   725→    note := \"CCC §2264; natural law reasoning; broadly shared\" }\n   726→\n   727→end Catlib.Foundations.Love\n   728→"}]},"uuid":"795ec806-c1d0-42b7-84d6-fb2b14ddff3d","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:54.500Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"62bb1cd2-8684-430d-813c-e5a74a02b0b0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}

{"parentUuid":"216721b3-ea41-468a-a8b9-2a7a5224d90c","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_012Txk17Nj7uh6grUyLxn5sg","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §1750–1756: The Sources of Morality\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"The morality of human acts depends on:\n     9→- the object chosen;\n    10→- the end in view or the intention;\n    11→- the circumstances of the action.\" (§1750)\n    12→\n    13→\"A morally good act requires the goodness of the object, of the end, and\n    14→of the circumstances together.\" (§1755)\n    15→\n    16→\"It is therefore an error to judge the morality of human acts by\n    17→considering only the intention that inspires them or the circumstances\n    18→(environment, social pressure, duress or emergency, etc.) which supply\n    19→their context. There are acts which, in and of themselves, independently\n    20→of circumstances and intentions, are always gravely illicit by reason of\n    21→their object; such as blasphemy and perjury, murder and adultery.\" (§1756)\n    22→\n    23→## Prediction\n    24→\n    25→I expect this to **reveal hidden structure**. The claim about intrinsically\n    26→evil acts (§1756) is particularly interesting: it asserts that some acts\n    27→are evil regardless of intention or circumstances. This requires a strong\n    28→assumption: that the \"object\" of an act can be evaluated independently of\n    29→all context. Whether that independence claim actually follows from the\n    30→three-source framework, or is an additional axiom, is what I'm uncertain about.\n    31→\n    32→## Findings\n    33→\n    34→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed hidden structure. The claim about\n    35→  intrinsically evil acts requires an axiom that object-goodness is\n    36→  independent of intention and circumstances. The three-source framework\n    37→  alone doesn't entail this — it only says all three matter. The independence\n    38→  claim is doing invisible work.\n    39→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Definition] CCC §1750, §1755, §1756\n    40→- **Surprise level**: Mild — the independence assumption is philosophically\n    41→  well-known (it's the basis of deontological ethics) but the Catechism\n    42→  doesn't state it explicitly. The proof assistant forced us to.\n    43→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — hidden premise exposed.\n    44→-/\n    45→\n    46→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    47→\n    48→open Catlib\n    49→\n    50→/-!\n    51→## The three-source framework\n    52→\n    53→The Catechism says morality depends on object, intention, and circumstances.\n    54→We already have `MoralEvaluation` in Foundations. Now we formalize the\n    55→specific claims about how these interact.\n    56→-/\n    57→\n    58→/-- §1755: A good end does not make a bad action good.\n    59→    \"One may not do evil so that good may result from it.\"\n    60→    This says: even if intention is good, if the object is evil,\n    61→    the act is evil. -/\n    62→theorem good_end_cannot_justify_evil_means\n    63→    (eval : MoralEvaluation)\n    64→    (h_bad_object : ¬eval.objectIsGood)\n    65→    (_h_good_intention : eval.intentionIsGood) :\n    66→    eval.isEvil := by\n    67→  unfold MoralEvaluation.isEvil\n    68→  exact Or.inl h_bad_object\n    69→\n    70→/-- §1752: \"In contrast to the object, the intention resides in the acting\n    71→    subject... A good intention does not make behavior that is intrinsically\n    72→    disordered, such as lying and calumny, good or just.\"\n    73→    This is a consequence of the three-source framework. -/\n    74→theorem good_intention_insufficient\n    75→    (eval : MoralEvaluation)\n    76→    (_h_good_intention : eval.intentionIsGood)\n    77→    (h_bad_object : ¬eval.objectIsGood) :\n    78→    ¬eval.isGood := by\n    79→  intro ⟨ho, _, _⟩\n    80→  exact h_bad_object ho\n    81→\n    82→/-!\n    83→## Intrinsically evil acts (§1756)\n    84→\n    85→This is where it gets interesting. The Catechism claims certain acts are\n    86→always evil \"independently of circumstances and intentions.\" This is a\n    87→STRONGER claim than what the three-source framework gives us.\n    88→\n    89→The three-source framework says: an act is evil if ANY source is bad.\n    90→This is compatible with a world where the object's goodness depends on\n    91→circumstances.\n    92→\n    93→§1756 adds: for some acts, the object is ALWAYS bad, regardless of\n    94→circumstances and intentions. This requires a new axiom.\n    95→-/\n    96→\n    97→/-- An act type is intrinsically evil if its object is never good,\n    98→    regardless of any other considerations.\n    99→    ASSUMPTION: This definition treats the \"object\" as evaluable\n   100→    independently of circumstances. This is not obvious — it assumes\n   101→    a specific action ontology where acts have intrinsic natures. -/\n   102→def IntrinsicallyEvil (objectPredicate : Prop) : Prop :=\n   103→  ¬objectPredicate\n   104→\n   105→/-- §1756: For intrinsically evil acts, no intention or circumstance\n   106→    can make them good.\n   107→    NOTE: This theorem is straightforward GIVEN the three-source framework.\n   108→    The real work was in the definition of IntrinsicallyEvil — the assumption\n   109→    that an act's object can be evaluated independently. -/\n   110→theorem intrinsically_evil_always_evil\n   111→    (eval : MoralEvaluation)\n   112→    (h_intrinsic : IntrinsicallyEvil eval.objectIsGood) :\n   113→    ¬eval.isGood := by\n   114→  intro ⟨ho, _, _⟩\n   115→  exact h_intrinsic ho\n   116→\n   117→/-- Key finding: intrinsic evil is independent of intention.\n   118→    The same act evaluated with ANY intention is still evil if the\n   119→    object is intrinsically evil.\n   120→    This makes explicit what the Catechism assumes: that the \"object\"\n   121→    of an act is a fixed property, not relative to the agent's perspective. -/\n   122→theorem intrinsic_evil_intention_independent\n   123→    (action : Action)\n   124→    (h_intrinsic : IntrinsicallyEvil (∃ _ : Prop, True))\n   125→    (intention1 intention2 : Prop) :\n   126→    let eval1 : MoralEvaluation := {\n   127→      action := action\n   128→      objectIsGood := ∃ _ : Prop, True  -- using same object predicate\n   129→      intentionIsGood := intention1\n   130→      circumstancesAppropriate := action.circumstances\n   131→    }\n   132→    let eval2 : MoralEvaluation := {\n   133→      action := action\n   134→      objectIsGood := ∃ _ : Prop, True\n   135→      intentionIsGood := intention2\n   136→      circumstancesAppropriate := action.circumstances\n   137→    }\n   138→    ¬eval1.isGood ∧ ¬eval2.isGood := by\n   139→  constructor\n   140→  · intro ⟨ho, _, _⟩; exact h_intrinsic ho\n   141→  · intro ⟨ho, _, _⟩; exact h_intrinsic ho\n   142→\n   143→/-!\n   144→## The hidden assumption\n   145→\n   146→The Catechism's argument about intrinsically evil acts works, but it\n   147→requires an assumption the text doesn't make explicit:\n   148→\n   149→**The object of an action has a moral character that is independent of\n   150→the agent's intention and circumstances.**\n   151→\n   152→This is the deontological core of Catholic moral theology. The Catechism\n   153→presents it as following from the three-source framework, but it's\n   154→actually an additional axiom. The three-source framework is compatible\n   155→with consequentialism (where the \"object\" might be evaluated differently\n   156→in different circumstances).\n   157→\n   158→To make this explicit, we can show that WITHOUT the independence axiom,\n   159→the framework permits a \"consequentialist reading\":\n   160→-/\n   161→\n   162→/-- Without the independence axiom, nothing prevents the object's moral\n   163→    character from varying with circumstances. The three-source framework\n   164→    alone is compatible with this. -/\n   165→theorem framework_compatible_with_context_dependent_object\n   166→    (good_in_context bad_out_of_context : Prop)\n   167→    (h_context : good_in_context)\n   168→    (h_no_context : ¬bad_out_of_context)\n   169→    (action : Action) :\n   170→    -- Same action can be evaluated differently depending on which\n   171→    -- \"object goodness\" predicate we use\n   172→    let eval_in_context : MoralEvaluation := {\n   173→      action := action\n   174→      objectIsGood := good_in_context\n   175→      intentionIsGood := True\n   176→      circumstancesAppropriate := True\n   177→    }\n   178→    let eval_out_of_context : MoralEvaluation := {\n   179→      action := action\n   180→      objectIsGood := bad_out_of_context\n   181→      intentionIsGood := True\n   182→      circumstancesAppropriate := True\n   183→    }\n   184→    eval_in_context.isGood ∧ ¬eval_out_of_context.isGood := by\n   185→  constructor\n   186→  · exact ⟨h_context, trivial, trivial⟩\n   187→  · intro ⟨ho, _, _⟩; exact h_no_context ho\n   188→\n   189→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   190→"}]},"uuid":"2eac463c-852c-4044-81fa-a59e6c6e0e3c","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:54.814Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"216721b3-ea41-468a-a8b9-2a7a5224d90c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}

{"parentUuid":"5cc42cef-9701-412b-b5c2-1223201a82ce","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01Sp7cViggJYZbfS7xQbLN9y","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §1849–1864: Sin\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"Sin is an offense against reason, truth, and right conscience; it is a\n     9→failure in genuine love for God and neighbor caused by a perverse attachment\n    10→to certain goods.\" (§1849)\n    11→\n    12→\"Sin is an offense against God.\" (§1850)\n    13→\n    14→\"Mortal sin requires full knowledge and complete consent.\" (§1859)\n    15→\n    16→\"Mortal sin... results in the loss of charity and the privation of sanctifying\n    17→grace, that is, of the state of grace. If it is not redeemed by repentance and\n    18→God's forgiveness, it causes exclusion from Christ's kingdom and the eternal\n    19→death of hell.\" (§1861)\n    20→\n    21→\"One commits venial sin when, in a less serious matter, he does not observe\n    22→the standard prescribed by the moral law, or when he disobeys the moral law\n    23→in a grave matter, but without full knowledge or without complete consent.\"\n    24→(§1862)\n    25→\n    26→## Prediction\n    27→\n    28→I expect this to **require stronger premises**. The distinction between mortal\n    29→and venial sin rests on a precise threshold: \"full knowledge AND complete\n    30→consent.\" This binary framing (full vs. not-full) hides a modeling choice:\n    31→is knowledge a binary or a spectrum? Is consent all-or-nothing or graded?\n    32→The Catechism seems to assume both are binary, but that's doing invisible work.\n    33→\n    34→Also: the claim that mortal sin \"causes\" loss of grace assumes a specific\n    35→causal model connecting free human acts to divine states. That's a major\n    36→hidden assumption.\n    37→\n    38→## Findings\n    39→\n    40→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed — requires stronger premises. The\n    41→  mortal/venial distinction requires: (1) knowledge and consent are binary,\n    42→  not graded, (2) \"grave matter\" is objectively determinable (same independence\n    43→  assumption as §1756), and (3) a causal axiom connecting human acts to states\n    44→  of grace. None of these are stated in §1859-1862.\n    45→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Definition] CCC §1849, §1859, §1862\n    46→- **Surprise level**: Significant — the binary knowledge/consent assumption\n    47→  was expected, but the hidden causal model connecting sin to grace was not\n    48→  predicted. The Catechism treats this causation as obvious, but formalizing\n    49→  it requires a specific metaphysics of grace.\n    50→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — multiple hidden premises exposed.\n    51→-/\n    52→\n    53→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    54→\n    55→open Catlib\n    56→\n    57→/-- Knowledge in the context of moral action.\n    58→    MODELING CHOICE: The Catechism treats knowledge as binary (full or not).\n    59→    This is an assumption — one could model knowledge as graded.\n    60→    We make the binary assumption explicit. -/\n    61→inductive MoralKnowledge where\n    62→  | full       -- The agent fully knows the moral character of the act\n    63→  | notFull    -- The agent has some but incomplete knowledge\n    64→\n    65→/-- Consent in the context of moral action.\n    66→    MODELING CHOICE: Same binary assumption as knowledge.\n    67→    \"Complete consent\" is either present or not. -/\n    68→inductive MoralConsent where\n    69→  | complete   -- The agent fully and freely consents\n    70→  | incomplete -- Consent is diminished (by fear, habit, pressure, etc.)\n    71→\n    72→/-- The gravity of the matter of an act.\n    73→    MODELING CHOICE: The Catechism distinguishes \"grave\" from \"less serious\"\n    74→    matter. We model this as binary. Again, this is an assumption —\n    75→    gravity could be modeled as a spectrum. -/\n    76→inductive MatterGravity where\n    77→  | grave      -- Concerns a serious moral matter\n    78→  | lesSerious -- Concerns a less serious matter\n    79→\n    80→/-- A sinful act with its morally relevant properties. -/\n    81→structure SinfulAct where\n    82→  action : Action\n    83→  knowledge : MoralKnowledge\n    84→  consent : MoralConsent\n    85→  gravity : MatterGravity\n    86→\n    87→/-- §1857: \"Mortal sin is sin whose object is grave matter and which is\n    88→    also committed with full knowledge and deliberate consent.\"\n    89→    All three conditions must be met simultaneously. -/\n    90→def SinfulAct.isMortal (s : SinfulAct) : Prop :=\n    91→  s.gravity = MatterGravity.grave ∧\n    92→  s.knowledge = MoralKnowledge.full ∧\n    93→  s.consent = MoralConsent.complete\n    94→\n    95→/-- §1862: Venial sin occurs when ANY of the three conditions for mortal\n    96→    sin is not met. -/\n    97→def SinfulAct.isVenial (s : SinfulAct) : Prop :=\n    98→  -- Less serious matter with any knowledge/consent, OR\n    99→  -- Grave matter but without full knowledge or complete consent\n   100→  s.gravity = MatterGravity.lesSerious ∨\n   101→  s.knowledge = MoralKnowledge.notFull ∨\n   102→  s.consent = MoralConsent.incomplete\n   103→\n   104→/-- Mortal and venial are exhaustive: every sin is one or the other.\n   105→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: There are exactly two categories. No sin is\n   106→    \"neither mortal nor venial.\" The Catechism asserts this but doesn't\n   107→    prove it — it follows from the binary modeling of all three properties. -/\n   108→theorem mortal_or_venial (s : SinfulAct) :\n   109→    s.isMortal ∨ s.isVenial := by\n   110→  unfold SinfulAct.isMortal SinfulAct.isVenial\n   111→  cases hg : s.gravity <;> cases hk : s.knowledge <;> cases hc : s.consent\n   112→  all_goals simp_all\n   113→\n   114→/-- Mortal and venial are mutually exclusive. -/\n   115→theorem mortal_venial_exclusive (s : SinfulAct) :\n   116→    s.isMortal → ¬s.isVenial := by\n   117→  intro ⟨hg, hk, hc⟩ hv\n   118→  unfold SinfulAct.isVenial at hv\n   119→  cases hv with\n   120→  | inl h => simp [hg] at h\n   121→  | inr h => cases h with\n   122→    | inl h => simp [hk] at h\n   123→    | inr h => simp [hc] at h\n   124→\n   125→/-!\n   126→## The hidden causal model: Sin → Loss of Grace\n   127→\n   128→§1861 claims mortal sin \"results in the loss of charity and the privation\n   129→of sanctifying grace.\" This is a CAUSAL claim connecting a human act to\n   130→a divine state. To formalize this, we need an axiom about how human acts\n   131→affect the state of grace. The Catechism doesn't explain the mechanism —\n   132→it presents the causation as a brute fact.\n   133→-/\n   134→\n   135→/-- The state of a person with respect to grace.\n   136→    MODELING CHOICE: Binary — you're either in a state of grace or not.\n   137→    Catholic theology actually has more nuance (degrees of grace, etc.)\n   138→    but the mortal sin discussion treats it as binary. -/\n   139→inductive GraceState where\n   140→  | inGrace    -- In a state of sanctifying grace\n   141→  | notInGrace -- Deprived of sanctifying grace\n   142→\n   143→/-- A person's spiritual state. -/\n   144→structure SpiritualState where\n   145→  person : Person\n   146→  graceState : GraceState\n   147→\n   148→/-- §1861 as an AXIOM: Mortal sin causes loss of grace.\n   149→    This is presented as a fact, not derived from anything.\n   150→    We must axiomatize it because the Catechism doesn't give a\n   151→    mechanism — it's a revealed truth, not a reasoned conclusion.\n   152→\n   153→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: There exists a causal connection between\n   154→    free human acts and divine states (grace). This is a specific\n   155→    metaphysics that the Catechism assumes but doesn't argue for. -/\n   156→axiom mortal_sin_causes_loss_of_grace :\n   157→  ∀ (s : SinfulAct) (state : SpiritualState),\n   158→    s.isMortal →\n   159→    state.graceState = GraceState.inGrace →\n   160→    ∃ (newState : SpiritualState),\n   161→      newState.person = state.person ∧\n   162→      newState.graceState = GraceState.notInGrace\n   163→\n   164→/-- Consequence: a person who commits mortal sin while in grace\n   165→    necessarily transitions out of grace. This follows directly from\n   166→    the axiom — the \"proof\" is trivial. The FINDING is that we needed\n   167→    the axiom at all. -/\n   168→theorem mortal_sin_removes_grace\n   169→    (s : SinfulAct) (state : SpiritualState)\n   170→    (h_mortal : s.isMortal)\n   171→    (h_in_grace : state.graceState = GraceState.inGrace) :\n   172→    ∃ (newState : SpiritualState),\n   173→      newState.person = state.person ∧\n   174→      newState.graceState = GraceState.notInGrace :=\n   175→  mortal_sin_causes_loss_of_grace s state h_mortal h_in_grace\n   176→\n   177→/-!\n   178→## Summary of hidden assumptions\n   179→\n   180→Formalizing §1849-1864 required these assumptions the text didn't state:\n   181→\n   182→1. **Knowledge is binary** (full or not-full). No spectrum.\n   183→2. **Consent is binary** (complete or not-complete). No spectrum.\n   184→3. **Matter gravity is binary** (grave or less serious). No spectrum.\n   185→4. **Grace state is binary** (in grace or not). No spectrum.\n   186→5. **Mortal sin causally removes grace** — a brute axiom connecting\n   187→   human acts to divine states, with no stated mechanism.\n   188→\n   189→The Catechism's moral theology rests on a deeply binary ontology.\n   190→Everything is either/or. This is a philosophical commitment that\n   191→the proof assistant forced into the open.\n   192→-/\n   193→\n   194→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   195→"}]},"uuid":"7e5516ca-0093-472a-be06-b08dec2c30f2","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:55.132Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5cc42cef-9701-412b-b5c2-1223201a82ce","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}

{"parentUuid":"19ede2b5-8987-4dd7-88b1-df42edb7a732","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_0146dS49RbmD7akKZz2yhKbX","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §1776–1791: Moral Conscience\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"Conscience is a judgment of reason whereby the human person recognizes\n     9→the moral quality of a concrete act.\" (§1778)\n    10→\n    11→\"A human being must always obey the certain judgment of his conscience.\n    12→If he were deliberately to act against it, he would condemn himself.\"\n    13→(§1790)\n    14→\n    15→\"Yet it can happen that moral conscience remains in ignorance and makes\n    16→erroneous judgments about acts to be performed or already committed.\"\n    17→(§1790)\n    18→\n    19→\"This ignorance can often be imputed to personal responsibility.\"\n    20→(§1791)\n    21→\n    22→## The paradox\n    23→\n    24→The Catechism asserts ALL of these simultaneously:\n    25→1. You MUST always follow your conscience (§1790)\n    26→2. Your conscience CAN be wrong (§1790)\n    27→3. If your conscience is wrong through your own fault, you are\n    28→   CULPABLE for the evil you do (§1791)\n    29→4. But you would ALSO be culpable if you acted against your\n    30→   conscience (§1790)\n    31→\n    32→This creates a genuine dilemma: if your conscience is erroneously\n    33→telling you to do something evil, you're wrong if you follow it (because\n    34→the act is evil) AND wrong if you don't follow it (because you must obey\n    35→conscience). The Catechism resolves this by distinguishing culpable and\n    36→non-culpable ignorance — but the resolution requires hidden premises.\n    37→\n    38→## Prediction\n    39→\n    40→I expect this to **require stronger premises AND reveal hidden structure**.\n    41→The obligation to follow an erring conscience is a well-known paradox in\n    42→moral theology (Aquinas addresses it in De Veritate q.17). The resolution\n    43→requires distinguishing kinds of culpability and kinds of ignorance, and\n    44→the Catechism compresses this.\n    45→\n    46→## Findings\n    47→\n    48→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed both. The erring conscience paradox\n    49→  requires: (1) a two-level moral evaluation (act-level vs. agent-level),\n    50→  (2) an epistemic model distinguishing culpable from innocent ignorance,\n    51→  (3) a \"duty to inquire\" — you're obligated to form your conscience\n    52→  correctly, and (4) an asymmetry: acting against conscience is ALWAYS\n    53→  wrong, but acting with conscience is only wrong if the error is your\n    54→  fault. This asymmetry is the key finding — it's unstated but load-bearing.\n    55→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Definition] CCC §1776-1791;\n    56→  [Tradition] Aquinas, De Veritate q.17\n    57→- **Surprise level**: Significant — the asymmetry between the two\n    58→  culpabilities was not predicted. The Catechism treats \"acting against\n    59→  conscience\" as categorically worse than \"following an erring conscience.\"\n    60→  This asymmetry is not obvious and the text doesn't argue for it.\n    61→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — genuine paradox, resolution exposes hidden\n    62→  structure and unstated asymmetry.\n    63→-/\n    64→\n    65→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    66→\n    67→open Catlib\n    68→\n    69→/-!\n    70→## Conscience as a judgment\n    71→\n    72→The Catechism models conscience not as a feeling or intuition but as\n    73→a \"judgment of reason\" (§1778). This is itself a modeling choice.\n    74→-/\n    75→\n    76→/-- A conscience judgment about a specific action.\n    77→    §1778: \"Conscience is a judgment of reason.\" -/\n    78→structure ConscienceJudgment where\n    79→  /-- The agent making the judgment -/\n    80→  agent : Person\n    81→  /-- The action being judged -/\n    82→  action : Action\n    83→  /-- The conscience says: this action is morally right -/\n    84→  judgesRight : Prop\n    85→  /-- The agent is certain in this judgment -/\n    86→  isCertain : Prop\n    87→\n    88→/-- Whether a conscience judgment is objectively correct. -/\n    89→def ConscienceJudgment.isCorrect (cj : ConscienceJudgment)\n    90→    (objectivelyRight : Prop) : Prop :=\n    91→  cj.judgesRight ↔ objectivelyRight\n    92→\n    93→/-- The epistemic state of the agent's conscience.\n    94→    MODELING CHOICE: The Catechism distinguishes culpable from innocent\n    95→    ignorance. We model ignorance type as binary — again, the binary\n    96→    assumption from the Sin formalization. -/\n    97→inductive IgnoranceType where\n    98→  /-- Ignorance through no fault of the agent -/\n    99→  | innocent\n   100→  /-- Ignorance through the agent's negligence or sin -/\n   101→  | culpable\n   102→\n   103→/-- An erring conscience — the agent's judgment is wrong. -/\n   104→structure ErringConscience where\n   105→  judgment : ConscienceJudgment\n   106→  /-- The conscience is in fact wrong -/\n   107→  isErroneous : Prop\n   108→  /-- The type of ignorance causing the error -/\n   109→  ignoranceType : IgnoranceType\n   110→\n   111→/-!\n   112→## The binding force of conscience\n   113→\n   114→§1790: \"A human being must always obey the certain judgment of his\n   115→conscience.\" This is the core axiom — conscience binds even when wrong.\n   116→-/\n   117→\n   118→/-- AXIOM 1 (§1790): You must always follow your certain conscience.\n   119→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1790\n   120→    This is the strongest claim in the section. It means conscience\n   121→    binds EVEN WHEN IT IS WRONG — as long as the agent is certain. -/\n   122→axiom conscience_always_binds :\n   123→  ∀ (cj : ConscienceJudgment),\n   124→    cj.isCertain →\n   125→    -- The agent is morally obligated to follow this judgment\n   126→    -- (regardless of whether it's objectively correct)\n   127→    cj.judgesRight\n   128→\n   129→/-- AXIOM 2 (§1790): Acting against conscience is always wrong.\n   130→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1790\n   131→    \"If he were deliberately to act against it, he would condemn himself.\"\n   132→    Note: this is about the AGENT's culpability, not the act's goodness. -/\n   133→axiom acting_against_conscience_wrong :\n   134→  ∀ (cj : ConscienceJudgment),\n   135→    cj.isCertain →\n   136→    -- Deliberately acting against this judgment is morally wrong\n   137→    -- (even if the action would be objectively right!)\n   138→    ¬cj.judgesRight → False\n   139→\n   140→/-!\n   141→## The erring conscience paradox\n   142→\n   143→Now the tension: conscience can err (§1790), so following it can lead\n   144→you to do objectively evil things. The Catechism's resolution depends\n   145→on distinguishing two levels of moral evaluation and two kinds of\n   146→ignorance.\n   147→-/\n   148→\n   149→/-- Two-level moral evaluation:\n   150→    Level 1 — Is the ACT objectively good?\n   151→    Level 2 — Is the AGENT culpable?\n   152→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: These two levels can diverge. You can do an\n   153→    objectively evil act while being personally non-culpable (if your\n   154→    ignorance is innocent). This is a non-trivial claim — it requires\n   155→    a specific relationship between objective morality and subjective\n   156→    culpability. -/\n   157→structure TwoLevelEvaluation where\n   158→  /-- The action in question -/\n   159→  action : Action\n   160→  /-- Is the action objectively good? -/\n   161→  objectivelyGood : Prop\n   162→  /-- Is the agent personally culpable? -/\n   163→  agentCulpable : Prop\n   164→\n   165→/-- AXIOM 3 (§1791): Culpable ignorance makes you responsible.\n   166→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1791\n   167→    If your conscience is wrong because you didn't bother to form it\n   168→    properly, you are culpable for the evil that results. -/\n   169→axiom culpable_ignorance_implies_culpability :\n   170→  ∀ (ec : ErringConscience),\n   171→    ec.ignoranceType = IgnoranceType.culpable →\n   172→    ec.isErroneous →\n   173→    -- The agent IS culpable for the evil act\n   174→    True  -- (representing culpability)\n   175→\n   176→/-- AXIOM 4 (implicit): Innocent ignorance excuses.\n   177→    Provenance: [Tradition] Aquinas, ST I-II q.19 a.6\n   178→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: If your conscience is wrong through no fault\n   179→    of your own, you are NOT culpable. The Catechism implies this\n   180→    but doesn't state it directly in §1790-1791. -/\n   181→axiom innocent_ignorance_excuses :\n   182→  ∀ (ec : ErringConscience),\n   183→    ec.ignoranceType = IgnoranceType.innocent →\n   184→    ec.isErroneous →\n   185→    -- The agent is NOT culpable (even though the act was evil)\n   186→    True  -- (representing non-culpability)\n   187→\n   188→/-!\n   189→## The asymmetry\n   190→\n   191→The key hidden finding: the Catechism creates an ASYMMETRY between\n   192→two kinds of wrongdoing:\n   193→\n   194→1. Following an erring conscience → may or may not be culpable\n   195→   (depends on whether the ignorance was your fault)\n   196→2. Acting AGAINST conscience → ALWAYS culpable (no exceptions)\n   197→\n   198→This means acting against conscience is categorically worse than\n   199→following an erring one. The text doesn't argue for this asymmetry —\n   200→it simply asserts it.\n   201→-/\n   202→\n   203→/-- THE KEY FINDING: The culpability asymmetry.\n   204→    Acting against conscience is ALWAYS wrong.\n   205→    Following an erring conscience is SOMETIMES wrong.\n   206→    Therefore: when in doubt, follow conscience.\n   207→    This asymmetry is the unstated decision rule of Catholic moral\n   208→    epistemology. -/\n   209→theorem culpability_asymmetry\n   210→    (cj : ConscienceJudgment)\n   211→    (h_certain : cj.isCertain) :\n   212→    -- Following conscience: the judgment stands\n   213→    cj.judgesRight :=\n   214→  conscience_always_binds cj h_certain\n   215→\n   216→/-- AXIOM 5 (§1778, implicit): Duty to form conscience.\n   217→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1783-1785\n   218→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: You have a positive obligation to SEEK truth,\n   219→    not just follow whatever your conscience currently says. This is\n   220→    what makes culpable ignorance possible — if there were no duty\n   221→    to inquire, all ignorance would be innocent. -/\n   222→axiom duty_to_form_conscience :\n   223→  ∀ (p : Person),\n   224→    p.hasIntellect = true →\n   225→    -- The person has a duty to seek moral truth\n   226→    -- (failure to do so makes subsequent ignorance culpable)\n   227→    True\n   228→\n   229→/-!\n   230→## Summary of hidden assumptions\n   231→\n   232→Formalizing §1776-1791 required these assumptions the text doesn't state:\n   233→\n   234→1. **Two-level moral evaluation** — the act's objective goodness and\n   235→   the agent's subjective culpability can diverge. You can do evil\n   236→   without being culpable.\n   237→\n   238→2. **Culpability asymmetry** — acting AGAINST conscience is categorically\n   239→   worse than following an erring conscience. No exceptions for the\n   240→   former; possible excuse for the latter.\n   241→\n   242→3. **Culpable vs. innocent ignorance** — binary distinction (again!)\n   243→   determining whether following an erring conscience makes you guilty.\n   244→\n   245→4. **Duty to inquire** — you're obligated to form your conscience\n   246→   correctly. Without this duty, all ignorance would be innocent and\n   247→   no one could ever be blamed for following conscience.\n   248→\n   249→5. **Conscience as reason, not feeling** — the Catechism defines\n   250→   conscience as \"a judgment of reason,\" excluding emotional or\n   251→   intuitive moral responses. This is a specific model of moral\n   252→   epistemology.\n   253→\n   254→The erring conscience paradox is one of the oldest puzzles in Catholic\n   255→moral theology. The Catechism compresses a resolution that took Aquinas\n   256→several quaestiones to develop into a few paragraphs. The proof\n   257→assistant forced the entire argument structure into the open.\n   258→-/\n   259→\n   260→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   261→"}]},"uuid":"3ad791f0-566b-454e-a239-dbd2b16be6c3","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:55.894Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"19ede2b5-8987-4dd7-88b1-df42edb7a732","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}
