{"parentUuid":"be23339f-107c-43c1-b69c-b03cb37da4bd","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"progress","data":{"type":"hook_progress","hookEvent":"PostToolUse","hookName":"PostToolUse:Read","command":"callback"},"parentToolUseID":"toolu_014TvmAWhyT841dqzEDCxx9m","toolUseID":"toolu_014TvmAWhyT841dqzEDCxx9m","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:56.274Z","uuid":"87a1fc17-920e-4e9d-a12b-496662d8a36a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}
{"parentUuid":"be23339f-107c-43c1-b69c-b03cb37da4bd","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_014TvmAWhyT841dqzEDCxx9m","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §1730–1738: Freedom and Responsibility\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"Freedom is the power, rooted in reason and will, to act or not to act,\n     9→to do this or that, and so to perform deliberate actions on one's own\n    10→responsibility.\" (§1731)\n    11→\n    12→\"As long as freedom has not bound itself definitively to its ultimate\n    13→good which is God, there is the possibility of choosing between good\n    14→and evil.\" (§1732)\n    15→\n    16→\"The more one does what is good, the freer one becomes. There is no\n    17→true freedom except in the service of what is good and just. The choice\n    18→to disobey and do evil is an abuse of freedom and leads to 'the slavery\n    19→of sin.'\" (§1733)\n    20→\n    21→\"Freedom makes man responsible for his acts to the extent that they are\n    22→voluntary.\" (§1734)\n    23→\n    24→## Two concepts of freedom in tension\n    25→\n    26→The Catechism contains TWO different concepts of freedom:\n    27→\n    28→1. **Freedom-as-choice** (§1731-1732): The ability to choose between\n    29→   alternatives — \"to act or not to act, to do this or that.\"\n    30→   This is what most modern people mean by \"freedom.\"\n    31→\n    32→2. **Freedom-as-flourishing** (§1733): \"The more one does what is good,\n    33→   the freer one becomes.\" Freedom is not just choice — it is the power\n    34→   to do what is genuinely good. Choosing evil DIMINISHES freedom.\n    35→\n    36→These two concepts are in tension. Under freedom-as-choice, choosing\n    37→evil is an exercise OF freedom. Under freedom-as-flourishing, choosing\n    38→evil is an abuse that reduces freedom. The Catechism holds both\n    39→simultaneously without explaining how they relate.\n    40→\n    41→## Prediction\n    42→\n    43→I expect this to **reveal hidden structure**. The two-concept tension\n    44→is well-known in political philosophy (Isaiah Berlin's \"negative\" vs.\n    45→\"positive\" liberty) but the Catechism doesn't acknowledge it as a\n    46→tension. Formalization should expose what model connects the two.\n    47→\n    48→## Findings\n    49→\n    50→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed — reveals hidden structure.\n    51→  The Catechism requires: (1) a teleological model of freedom — freedom\n    52→  has a PURPOSE (directed toward God), not just a capacity, (2) an\n    53→  ordering of choices — choosing good is MORE free than choosing evil,\n    54→  which requires a non-neutral metric, (3) freedom is graded, not\n    55→  binary — you can become MORE or LESS free, (4) the connection between\n    56→  freedom and responsibility requires that diminished freedom diminishes\n    57→  responsibility (§1734), and (5) perfect freedom is the INABILITY to\n    58→  choose evil — which means the freest being (God) cannot sin.\n    59→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Definition] CCC §1731-1734;\n    60→  [Tradition] Aquinas ST I-II q.1-5 (ultimate end), q.109 (grace and\n    61→  freedom)\n    62→- **Surprise level**: Significant — the most surprising finding is that\n    63→  PERFECT freedom = inability to sin. This is counterintuitive: most\n    64→  people think freedom MEANS the ability to choose evil. The Catechism\n    65→  says the opposite — the ability to choose evil is a DEFICIENCY of\n    66→  freedom, not its essence.\n    67→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — the teleological model of freedom and the\n    68→  \"perfect freedom = no evil\" finding are both genuinely surprising.\n    69→-/\n    70→\n    71→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    72→\n    73→open Catlib\n    74→\n    75→/-!\n    76→## Two models of freedom\n    77→-/\n    78→\n    79→/-- The degree of freedom a person has.\n    80→    MODELING CHOICE: Freedom is graded, not binary.\n    81→    The Catechism says you can become \"freer\" (§1733),\n    82→    which means freedom admits of degrees. -/\n    83→structure FreedomDegree where\n    84→  /-- How free is this person? (0 = enslaved, 1 = perfectly free) -/\n    85→  level : Nat  -- Using Nat for simplicity; ideally an ordered type\n    86→  /-- Is this person currently able to choose between good and evil? -/\n    87→  canChooseEvil : Prop\n    88→  /-- Is this person oriented toward the good? -/\n    89→  orientedToGood : Prop\n    90→\n    91→/-- Freedom-as-choice: the modern/liberal concept.\n    92→    Freedom = having options. More options = more freedom.\n    93→    Choosing evil is an EXERCISE of freedom. -/\n    94→def freedomAsChoice (canChoose : Prop) : Prop := canChoose\n    95→\n    96→/-- Freedom-as-flourishing: the Catechism's deeper concept.\n    97→    Freedom = power to do what is genuinely good.\n    98→    Choosing evil DIMINISHES freedom. -/\n    99→def freedomAsFlourishing (orientedToGood : Prop) : Prop := orientedToGood\n   100→\n   101→/-- AXIOM 1 (§1731): Freedom is rooted in reason and will.\n   102→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1731\n   103→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Freedom requires BOTH reason and will.\n   104→    A being with will but no reason (an animal?) is not free.\n   105→    A being with reason but no will (a computer?) is not free.\n   106→    Freedom is specifically the intersection of these capacities. -/\n   107→axiom freedom_requires_reason_and_will :\n   108→  ∀ (p : Person),\n   109→    p.hasIntellect = true →\n   110→    p.hasFreeWill = true →\n   111→    -- This person has genuine freedom\n   112→    True\n   113→\n   114→/-- AXIOM 2 (§1733): Doing good increases freedom.\n   115→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1733\n   116→    \"The more one does what is good, the freer one becomes.\"\n   117→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: This is a TELEOLOGICAL claim — freedom has\n   118→    a direction. It's not neutral between good and evil choices.\n   119→    This contradicts the common modern assumption that freedom is\n   120→    just \"having options.\" Under the Catechism, some options make\n   121→    you MORE free and others make you LESS free. -/\n   122→axiom good_increases_freedom :\n   123→  ∀ (fd : FreedomDegree),\n   124→    fd.orientedToGood →\n   125→    -- Freedom level increases\n   126→    -- (choosing good makes you freer)\n   127→    fd.level > 0\n   128→\n   129→/-- AXIOM 3 (§1733): Choosing evil diminishes freedom.\n   130→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1733\n   131→    \"The choice to disobey and do evil is an abuse of freedom\n   132→    and leads to 'the slavery of sin.'\"\n   133→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Evil is self-undermining. The more evil you\n   134→    choose, the less capable you become of choosing good. This is\n   135→    a specific empirical-metaphysical claim about the effects of\n   136→    moral choices on the agent's capacities. -/\n   137→axiom evil_diminishes_freedom :\n   138→  ∀ (fd : FreedomDegree),\n   139→    ¬fd.orientedToGood →\n   140→    -- Choosing evil leads toward slavery\n   141→    -- (the agent becomes less free)\n   142→    True\n   143→\n   144→/-- AXIOM 4 (§1732): The possibility of evil is a feature of\n   145→    IMPERFECT freedom, not freedom itself.\n   146→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1732\n   147→    \"As long as freedom has not bound itself definitively to its\n   148→    ultimate good... there is the possibility of choosing between\n   149→    good and evil.\"\n   150→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: The ability to choose evil is a TRANSITIONAL\n   151→    property — it characterizes freedom on the way to perfection,\n   152→    not freedom at its peak. -/\n   153→axiom evil_possible_only_in_imperfect_freedom :\n   154→  ∀ (fd : FreedomDegree),\n   155→    fd.canChooseEvil →\n   156→    -- Freedom is not yet perfected\n   157→    -- (a perfectly free being CANNOT choose evil)\n   158→    ¬fd.orientedToGood ∨ fd.orientedToGood\n   159→    -- (this is trivially true but the conceptual point is:\n   160→    -- canChooseEvil implies not-yet-perfected)\n   161→\n   162→/-!\n   163→## The perfect freedom paradox\n   164→\n   165→The most counterintuitive consequence: PERFECT freedom is the\n   166→INABILITY to choose evil.\n   167→\n   168→Most people think: freedom = ability to choose anything.\n   169→The Catechism says: freedom = ability to do what is genuinely good.\n   170→A being who can only choose good is MORE free than one who can\n   171→choose either good or evil.\n   172→\n   173→This means God (who cannot sin) is the FREEST being — not despite\n   174→being unable to sin, but BECAUSE of it.\n   175→-/\n   176→\n   177→/-- Perfect freedom: fully oriented to good, no longer able to\n   178→    choose evil. This is the state of the blessed in heaven\n   179→    and (always) of God. -/\n   180→structure PerfectFreedom where\n   181→  person : Person\n   182→  /-- Fully oriented to the good -/\n   183→  fullyOriented : Prop\n   184→  /-- Can no longer choose evil -/\n   185→  cannotChooseEvil : Prop\n   186→\n   187→/-- The perfect freedom paradox: under the Catechism's model,\n   188→    the inability to choose evil IS the perfection of freedom,\n   189→    not its negation.\n   190→    Under the modern model, this being would be UNFREE (no choice).\n   191→    Under the Catechism's model, this being is MOST FREE.\n   192→    These are incompatible models that the text never distinguishes. -/\n   193→theorem perfect_freedom_cannot_sin\n   194→    (pf : PerfectFreedom)\n   195→    (h_oriented : pf.fullyOriented)\n   196→    (h_cannot : pf.cannotChooseEvil) :\n   197→    -- This being is both fully free AND unable to sin\n   198→    pf.fullyOriented ∧ pf.cannotChooseEvil :=\n   199→  ⟨h_oriented, h_cannot⟩\n   200→\n   201→/-!\n   202→## Freedom and responsibility\n   203→\n   204→§1734: \"Freedom makes man responsible for his acts to the extent\n   205→that they are voluntary.\" This introduces proportional responsibility\n   206→— another graded concept.\n   207→-/\n   208→\n   209→/-- AXIOM 5 (§1734): Responsibility is proportional to freedom.\n   210→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1734\n   211→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: If freedom is graded (you can be more or less\n   212→    free), and responsibility tracks freedom, then responsibility is\n   213→    also graded. A person acting under diminished freedom has\n   214→    diminished responsibility. This connects back to the Sin\n   215→    formalization — the \"full knowledge and complete consent\"\n   216→    requirement for mortal sin is a threshold on this spectrum. -/\n   217→axiom responsibility_proportional_to_freedom :\n   218→  ∀ (fd : FreedomDegree) (p : Person),\n   219→    p.hasFreeWill = true →\n   220→    -- Responsibility is proportional to the degree of freedom\n   221→    -- (more free = more responsible)\n   222→    fd.level > 0 → True\n   223→\n   224→/-!\n   225→## Summary of hidden assumptions\n   226→\n   227→Formalizing §1730-1738 required these assumptions the text doesn't state:\n   228→\n   229→1. **Teleological freedom** — freedom has a direction. It's oriented\n   230→   toward God/the good. This is NOT neutral choice between options.\n   231→\n   232→2. **Freedom is graded** — you can become more or less free. Choosing\n   233→   good increases freedom; choosing evil diminishes it.\n   234→\n   235→3. **Evil is self-undermining** — the more evil you choose, the less\n   236→   capable you become of choosing good. Empirical-metaphysical claim.\n   237→\n   238→4. **Perfect freedom = inability to sin** — the freest being cannot\n   239→   choose evil. This contradicts the common assumption that freedom\n   240→   means having all options open.\n   241→\n   242→5. **Proportional responsibility** — if freedom is graded, so is moral\n   243→   responsibility. Diminished freedom = diminished responsibility.\n   244→\n   245→6. **Two concepts held simultaneously** — freedom-as-choice (§1731)\n   246→   and freedom-as-flourishing (§1733) coexist without the text\n   247→   explaining their relationship.\n   248→\n   249→The Catechism's concept of freedom is radically different from the\n   250→modern liberal concept. Where modern thought says \"freedom = options,\"\n   251→the Catechism says \"freedom = power to do good.\" This is the deepest\n   252→philosophical commitment in the moral theology sections, and the one\n   253→most likely to surprise a modern reader.\n   254→-/\n   255→\n   256→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   257→"}]},"uuid":"ef36c0ee-ae68-42ad-b52d-825ae24d8ff4","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:39:56.273Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"be23339f-107c-43c1-b69c-b03cb37da4bd","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}

{"parentUuid":"7401537b-e515-45b5-a17c-167b0e4f542c","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_016NJhEFj3YKtHhRkVMJH9br","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §1954–1957: The Natural Moral Law\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"The natural law expresses the original moral sense which enables man\n     9→to discern by reason the good and the evil.\" (§1954)\n    10→\n    11→\"The natural law, present in the heart of each man and established by\n    12→reason, is universal in its precepts and its authority extends to all\n    13→men. It is... immutable and eternal.\" (§1956)\n    14→\n    15→\"Application of the natural law varies greatly; it can demand reflection\n    16→that takes account of various conditions of life according to places,\n    17→times, and circumstances. Nevertheless, in the diversity of cultures,\n    18→the natural law remains as a rule that binds men among themselves and\n    19→imposes on them, beyond the inevitable differences, common principles.\"\n    20→(§1957)\n    21→\n    22→## The tension\n    23→\n    24→§1956 says the natural law is universal and immutable — the same for\n    25→everyone, everywhere, always. §1957 says application \"varies greatly\"\n    26→by place, time, and circumstances. This creates a structural tension:\n    27→\n    28→If the law is truly immutable, what exactly is \"varying\"? There must be\n    29→a distinction between the LAW ITSELF and its APPLICATION. This distinction\n    30→is not trivial — it requires a specific model of how abstract moral\n    31→principles relate to concrete situations.\n    32→\n    33→## Prediction\n    34→\n    35→I expect this to **require stronger premises**. The universality claim\n    36→(\"extends to all men\") requires that reason is sufficient to discover\n    37→moral truth — but what counts as \"reason\"? The immutability claim\n    38→requires a specific metaphysics of moral facts. And the tension between\n    39→§1956 and §1957 will force a distinction between law and application\n    40→that the text leaves implicit.\n    41→\n    42→The most interesting question: what does \"accessible to reason\" actually\n    43→mean? If two people reason honestly and disagree about a moral precept,\n    44→does natural law theory say one of them is failing to reason properly?\n    45→That's a strong claim the text never makes explicit.\n    46→\n    47→## Findings\n    48→\n    49→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed — requires stronger premises.\n    50→  Five hidden axioms: (1) moral realism (moral facts exist independently\n    51→  of human opinion), (2) rational accessibility (reason alone can discover\n    52→  these facts), (3) law/application distinction (the law is immutable but\n    53→  its application to concrete cases varies), (4) rational convergence\n    54→  (all reasoners SHOULD reach the same moral conclusions — if they don't,\n    55→  someone is reasoning badly), (5) divine grounding (natural law has the\n    56→  \"force of law\" only because it's the voice of a \"higher reason\").\n    57→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Natural Law] Aquinas, Summa I-II\n    58→  q.94; [Definition] CCC §1954, §1956, §1957\n    59→- **Surprise level**: Significant — the rational convergence assumption\n    60→  was not predicted. The Catechism assumes that disagreement about natural\n    61→  law is always a failure of reason, never a feature of genuine moral\n    62→  complexity. This is a very strong claim hidden in the word \"universal.\"\n    63→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — multiple hidden premises, the rational\n    64→  convergence assumption is genuinely surprising.\n    65→-/\n    66→\n    67→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    68→\n    69→open Catlib\n    70→\n    71→/-!\n    72→## The structure of natural law\n    73→\n    74→The Catechism describes natural law as having two layers:\n    75→1. The law itself — universal, immutable, eternal\n    76→2. Its application — varies by place, time, circumstances\n    77→\n    78→This two-layer structure is not stated explicitly but is required\n    79→to make §1956 and §1957 compatible.\n    80→-/\n    81→\n    82→/-- A moral precept — a principle of the natural law.\n    83→    These are the immutable, universal content of the law. -/\n    84→structure MoralPrecept where\n    85→  /-- What the precept prescribes or forbids -/\n    86→  content : Prop\n    87→  /-- The precept is discoverable by reason alone -/\n    88→  accessibleToReason : Prop\n    89→  /-- The precept applies to all persons -/\n    90→  universal : Prop\n    91→\n    92→/-- A concrete moral situation in which precepts must be applied.\n    93→    §1957: Application depends on \"places, times, and circumstances.\" -/\n    94→structure MoralContext where\n    95→  /-- The cultural/geographical setting -/\n    96→  place : Type\n    97→  /-- The historical period -/\n    98→  time : Type\n    99→  /-- Relevant circumstances -/\n   100→  circumstances : Type\n   101→\n   102→/-- An application of a precept to a concrete situation.\n   103→    This is what §1957 says \"varies greatly.\" -/\n   104→structure Application where\n   105→  precept : MoralPrecept\n   106→  context : MoralContext\n   107→  /-- The concrete moral judgment in this context -/\n   108→  judgment : Prop\n   109→\n   110→/-- AXIOM 1 (§1954): Moral realism — moral facts exist independently\n   111→    of human opinion.\n   112→    Provenance: [Natural Law] — assumed throughout\n   113→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: This is the deepest premise of natural law theory.\n   114→    If moral \"facts\" are just human conventions, natural law collapses.\n   115→    The Catechism never argues for moral realism — it assumes it. -/\n   116→axiom moral_realism :\n   117→  ∀ (p : MoralPrecept), p.content ∨ ¬p.content\n   118→  -- Moral precepts have definite truth values\n   119→  -- (This is just excluded middle for Props, but the philosophical\n   120→  -- claim is that moral propositions ARE the kind of thing that\n   121→  -- can be true or false — not just expressions of preference)\n   122→\n   123→/-- AXIOM 2 (§1954): Rational accessibility — reason alone can\n   124→    discover the natural law.\n   125→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1954\n   126→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: \"Reason\" is sufficient for moral knowledge.\n   127→    But what counts as reason? If two people reason honestly and disagree,\n   128→    the theory says one of them is failing to reason properly. That's a\n   129→    strong claim the text never makes explicit. -/\n   130→axiom rational_accessibility :\n   131→  ∀ (p : MoralPrecept) (person : Person),\n   132→    person.hasIntellect = true →\n   133→    -- The person CAN (in principle) discover this precept\n   134→    p.accessibleToReason\n   135→\n   136→/-- AXIOM 3 (§1956): Universality — the natural law's precepts apply\n   137→    to all humans.\n   138→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1956\n   139→    \"Its authority extends to all men.\" -/\n   140→axiom universality :\n   141→  ∀ (p : MoralPrecept) (person : Person),\n   142→    person.hasIntellect = true →\n   143→    person.hasFreeWill = true →\n   144→    p.universal\n   145→\n   146→/-- AXIOM 4 (§1956 + §1957): The law/application distinction.\n   147→    The law is immutable, but its application varies.\n   148→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1956, §1957\n   149→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: There IS a clean separation between the abstract\n   150→    precept and its concrete application. This is not obvious — one could\n   151→    argue that a \"law\" that requires completely different actions in\n   152→    different contexts is not really the same law. The Catechism assumes\n   153→    the separation is clean. -/\n   154→axiom law_application_distinction :\n   155→  ∀ (p : MoralPrecept) (_ctx1 _ctx2 : MoralContext)\n   156→    (app1 : Application) (app2 : Application),\n   157→    app1.precept = p →\n   158→    app2.precept = p →\n   159→    -- The precept is the same even if the judgments differ\n   160→    app1.precept.content = app2.precept.content\n   161→\n   162→/-- AXIOM 5 (§1954): Divine grounding — natural law has the force of\n   163→    law because it participates in a \"higher reason.\"\n   164→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1954\n   165→    \"This command of human reason would not have the force of law if it\n   166→    were not the voice and interpreter of a higher reason.\"\n   167→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Without God, there is no natural law. This means\n   168→    natural law theory is not really \"accessible to reason alone\" — it\n   169→    requires belief in (or at least the existence of) a divine lawgiver.\n   170→    This is a tension the Catechism doesn't resolve. -/\n   171→axiom divine_grounding :\n   172→  -- Natural law has binding force BECAUSE it comes from God\n   173→  -- Without this axiom, natural law is just \"what reason discovers\"\n   174→  -- which lacks the normative force of LAW\n   175→  ∀ (p : MoralPrecept),\n   176→    p.accessibleToReason →\n   177→    -- The precept has binding moral authority\n   178→    p.content\n   179→\n   180→/-!\n   181→## The rational convergence problem\n   182→\n   183→The deepest hidden assumption: if natural law is universal AND\n   184→accessible to reason, then all honest reasoners SHOULD converge on\n   185→the same moral conclusions. Persistent disagreement must be a failure\n   186→of reason, not a genuine feature of moral reality.\n   187→\n   188→This is an extraordinarily strong claim.\n   189→-/\n   190→\n   191→/-- Two reasoners examining the same precept. -/\n   192→structure TwoReasoners where\n   193→  person1 : Person\n   194→  person2 : Person\n   195→  precept : MoralPrecept\n   196→  h1_intellect : person1.hasIntellect = true\n   197→  h2_intellect : person2.hasIntellect = true\n   198→\n   199→/-- The rational convergence claim: both reasoners can discover the\n   200→    same moral truth. If they disagree, at least one is failing to\n   201→    reason properly.\n   202→    This follows from rational_accessibility + universality, but the\n   203→    philosophical commitment it represents is much stronger than either\n   204→    axiom alone. -/\n   205→theorem rational_convergence (r : TwoReasoners) :\n   206→    r.precept.accessibleToReason :=\n   207→  rational_accessibility r.precept r.person1 r.h1_intellect\n   208→\n   209→/-!\n   210→## The divine grounding tension\n   211→\n   212→§1954 creates a structural tension:\n   213→- Natural law is \"accessible to reason\" (no revelation needed)\n   214→- Natural law \"would not have the force of law\" without being\n   215→  \"the voice of a higher reason\" (God required)\n   216→\n   217→So: you can DISCOVER the law by reason, but the law only EXISTS\n   218→because of God. This means an atheist can know the natural law\n   219→but cannot account for WHY it binds them.\n   220→\n   221→This raises a question: is natural law theory really \"natural\"\n   222→(i.e., independent of revelation), or does it secretly require theism?\n   223→-/\n   224→\n   225→/-- The structural tension: accessible to reason, but grounded in God. -/\n   226→theorem natural_law_tension\n   227→    (p : MoralPrecept)\n   228→    (person : Person)\n   229→    (h_intellect : person.hasIntellect = true) :\n   230→    -- The precept is accessible to this person's reason\n   231→    p.accessibleToReason :=\n   232→  rational_accessibility p person h_intellect\n   233→\n   234→/-- But its binding force comes from divine grounding, not from\n   235→    reason alone. An atheist can discover the precept but (on this\n   236→    view) cannot fully account for why it binds. -/\n   237→theorem binding_force_requires_god\n   238→    (p : MoralPrecept)\n   239→    (h_accessible : p.accessibleToReason) :\n   240→    -- The precept actually holds (has binding force)\n   241→    p.content :=\n   242→  divine_grounding p h_accessible\n   243→\n   244→/-!\n   245→## The immutability problem\n   246→\n   247→§1956 says natural law is \"immutable and eternal.\" But §1957 says\n   248→application varies. The law_application_distinction axiom handles this,\n   249→but it raises a deeper question:\n   250→\n   251→If you can never observe the law directly — only its applications,\n   252→which vary — what does \"immutable\" even mean? An immutable law whose\n   253→applications always change looks indistinguishable from no law at all,\n   254→unless you accept the metaphysical claim that the abstract precept\n   255→EXISTS independently of all its applications.\n   256→\n   257→This is Platonic realism about moral laws — another hidden assumption.\n   258→-/\n   259→\n   260→/-- The immutability claim: the precept's content doesn't change. -/\n   261→theorem immutability (p : MoralPrecept)\n   262→    (ctx1 ctx2 : MoralContext)\n   263→    (app1 app2 : Application)\n   264→    (h1 : app1.precept = p)\n   265→    (h2 : app2.precept = p) :\n   266→    app1.precept.content = app2.precept.content :=\n   267→  law_application_distinction p ctx1 ctx2 app1 app2 h1 h2\n   268→\n   269→/-!\n   270→## Summary of hidden assumptions\n   271→\n   272→Formalizing §1954-1957 required these assumptions the text doesn't state:\n   273→\n   274→1. **Moral realism** — moral facts exist independently of human opinion.\n   275→   Without this, \"natural law\" is just \"widely shared moral intuitions.\"\n   276→\n   277→2. **Rational accessibility** — reason alone can discover moral truth.\n   278→   But what counts as \"proper\" reasoning? The theory must say disagreement\n   279→   is always a failure of reason.\n   280→\n   281→3. **Rational convergence** — all honest reasoners should converge on the\n   282→   same conclusions. Persistent disagreement = someone is reasoning badly.\n   283→   This is an extraordinarily strong claim hidden in \"universal.\"\n   284→\n   285→4. **Law/application distinction** — the law is immutable even though its\n   286→   applications vary. This requires Platonic realism about moral laws.\n   287→\n   288→5. **Divine grounding** — natural law only has the \"force of law\" because\n   289→   it comes from God. So natural law is discoverable by reason but\n   290→   grounded in revelation — a tension the Catechism doesn't resolve.\n   291→\n   292→The natural law section of the Catechism does more philosophical heavy\n   293→lifting per paragraph than almost any other section. Five major\n   294→metaphysical commitments in four paragraphs, none stated explicitly.\n   295→-/\n   296→\n   297→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   298→"}]},"uuid":"35e46ad2-5d16-47ca-b69d-40cfeb913564","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:40:09.099Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7401537b-e515-45b5-a17c-167b0e4f542c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}
{"parentUuid":"35e46ad2-5d16-47ca-b69d-40cfeb913564","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"progress","data":{"type":"hook_progress","hookEvent":"PostToolUse","hookName":"PostToolUse:Read","command":"callback"},"parentToolUseID":"toolu_013nUBKzZzcKGWjrLcTgcdjC","toolUseID":"toolu_013nUBKzZzcKGWjrLcTgcdjC","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:40:09.109Z","uuid":"ead1e85d-3200-4ab2-a3a0-ff7b2c2436d2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}
{"parentUuid":"a9843526-86cc-47e5-b429-8f6c79ed7c2a","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_013nUBKzZzcKGWjrLcTgcdjC","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §2263–2267: Legitimate Defense\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"The act of self-defense can have a double effect: the preservation of\n     9→one's own life; and the killing of the aggressor.\" (§2263)\n    10→\n    11→\"Someone who defends his life is not guilty of murder even if he is\n    12→forced to deal his aggressor a lethal blow... if he repels force with\n    13→moderation, his defense will be lawful.\" (§2264)\n    14→\n    15→\"Legitimate defense can be not only a right but a grave duty for one\n    16→who is responsible for the lives of others.\" (§2265)\n    17→\n    18→## The double effect doctrine\n    19→\n    20→§2263 applies the principle of double effect — one of the most important\n    21→and debated principles in Catholic moral theology. An act with both a\n    22→good effect (saving your life) and a bad effect (killing the aggressor)\n    23→can be morally permissible IF certain conditions are met.\n    24→\n    25→## Prediction\n    26→\n    27→I expect this to **reveal hidden structure**. The double effect doctrine\n    28→has four standard conditions (from Aquinas/Mangan). The Catechism states\n    29→some but not all of them explicitly. Formalization should expose which\n    30→conditions are doing invisible work.\n    31→\n    32→Also: the proportionality requirement (\"moderate self-defense\") assumes\n    33→the defender can ACCURATELY ASSESS the threat level. This is an epistemic\n    34→assumption about knowledge in crisis situations that the text never\n    35→addresses.\n    36→\n    37→## Findings\n    38→\n    39→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed — reveals hidden structure.\n    40→  The double effect doctrine requires FOUR conditions (the standard\n    41→  formulation), but §2263-2264 only states TWO explicitly (good\n    42→  intention and proportionality). The other two (the act must not be\n    43→  intrinsically evil, and the good effect must not come THROUGH the bad\n    44→  effect) are assumed. Also: proportionality assumes accurate threat\n    45→  assessment — a significant epistemic assumption.\n    46→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Tradition] Aquinas, ST II-II q.64\n    47→  a.7; [Definition] CCC §2263-2265\n    48→- **Surprise level**: Significant — the epistemic assumption about\n    49→  threat assessment was not predicted. The Catechism's proportionality\n    50→  requirement assumes perfect (or near-perfect) knowledge of the threat,\n    51→  which is exactly what people lack in self-defense situations.\n    52→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — the epistemic gap is a genuine finding.\n    53→-/\n    54→\n    55→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    56→\n    57→open Catlib\n    58→\n    59→/-!\n    60→## The Principle of Double Effect\n    61→\n    62→The standard formulation (from Mangan 1949, based on Aquinas) has\n    63→four conditions. An act with both good and bad effects is permissible\n    64→if and only if ALL FOUR hold.\n    65→-/\n    66→\n    67→/-- An act that produces both good and bad effects. -/\n    68→structure DoubleEffectAct where\n    69→  /-- The agent -/\n    70→  agent : Person\n    71→  /-- The good effect (e.g., saving one's life) -/\n    72→  goodEffect : Prop\n    73→  /-- The bad effect (e.g., death of the aggressor) -/\n    74→  badEffect : Prop\n    75→  /-- The agent intends the good effect -/\n    76→  intendsGood : Prop\n    77→  /-- The agent does NOT intend the bad effect -/\n    78→  doesNotIntendBad : Prop\n    79→\n    80→/-- The four conditions of the principle of double effect. -/\n    81→structure DoubleEffectConditions where\n    82→  act : DoubleEffectAct\n    83→  /-- Condition 1: The act itself is not intrinsically evil.\n    84→      (§2263 assumes this — self-defense is not intrinsically evil.) -/\n    85→  actNotIntrinsicallyEvil : Prop\n    86→  /-- Condition 2: The agent intends the good effect, not the bad.\n    87→      (§2263 states this: preserving one's life is the intention.) -/\n    88→  goodIntended : Prop\n    89→  /-- Condition 3: The bad effect is not the MEANS to the good.\n    90→      The good must not come THROUGH the bad effect.\n    91→      (NOT explicitly stated in §2263-2264.) -/\n    92→  badNotMeansToGood : Prop\n    93→  /-- Condition 4: Proportionality — the good effect outweighs the bad.\n    94→      (§2264 states this: \"moderate self-defense.\") -/\n    95→  proportionate : Prop\n    96→\n    97→/-- The principle of double effect: all four conditions must hold. -/\n    98→def DoubleEffectConditions.isPermissible (dec : DoubleEffectConditions) : Prop :=\n    99→  dec.actNotIntrinsicallyEvil ∧\n   100→  dec.goodIntended ∧\n   101→  dec.badNotMeansToGood ∧\n   102→  dec.proportionate\n   103→\n   104→/-- AXIOM 1 (§2263): Self-defense has double effect.\n   105→    Provenance: [Tradition] Aquinas ST II-II q.64 a.7\n   106→    \"The act of self-defense can have a double effect: the preservation\n   107→    of one's own life; and the killing of the aggressor.\" -/\n   108→axiom self_defense_double_effect :\n   109→  ∀ (act : DoubleEffectAct),\n   110→    act.goodEffect → act.badEffect →\n   111→    act.intendsGood →\n   112→    -- The act has genuine double effect\n   113→    act.intendsGood\n   114→\n   115→/-- AXIOM 2 (§2264): Proportionality is required.\n   116→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §2264; [Tradition] Aquinas\n   117→    \"If a man in self-defense uses more than necessary violence,\n   118→    it will be unlawful.\"\n   119→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: The defender can accurately assess what\n   120→    counts as \"necessary\" violence. In real self-defense situations,\n   121→    the defender has imperfect information about the threat level.\n   122→    The proportionality requirement assumes epistemic access the\n   123→    defender may not have. -/\n   124→axiom proportionality_required :\n   125→  ∀ (dec : DoubleEffectConditions),\n   126→    dec.proportionate →\n   127→    -- The response is proportional to the threat\n   128→    True\n   129→\n   130→/-!\n   131→## The epistemic gap\n   132→\n   133→The Catechism's proportionality requirement assumes the defender can\n   134→assess the threat accurately. But self-defense happens in crisis\n   135→conditions: fear, adrenaline, imperfect information, split-second\n   136→decisions. The text never addresses what happens when a defender\n   137→genuinely believed the threat was lethal but it wasn't.\n   138→-/\n   139→\n   140→/-- The defender's epistemic state during a threat. -/\n   141→structure ThreatAssessment where\n   142→  /-- The actual threat level -/\n   143→  actualThreat : Nat  -- 0 = none, higher = more severe\n   144→  /-- The defender's perceived threat level -/\n   145→  perceivedThreat : Nat\n   146→  /-- Whether the assessment is accurate -/\n   147→  isAccurate : Prop\n   148→\n   149→/-- A defense action with its epistemic context. -/\n   150→structure DefenseAction where\n   151→  defender : Person\n   152→  assessment : ThreatAssessment\n   153→  /-- The force used in defense -/\n   154→  forceUsed : Nat\n   155→\n   156→/-- Proportionality under ACTUAL threat — what the Catechism assumes. -/\n   157→def proportionalToActual (da : DefenseAction) : Prop :=\n   158→  da.forceUsed ≤ da.assessment.actualThreat\n   159→\n   160→/-- Proportionality under PERCEIVED threat — what the defender can\n   161→    actually calibrate to. -/\n   162→def proportionalToPerceived (da : DefenseAction) : Prop :=\n   163→  da.forceUsed ≤ da.assessment.perceivedThreat\n   164→\n   165→/-- THE EPISTEMIC GAP: The Catechism requires proportionality to the\n   166→    actual threat, but the defender can only calibrate to the perceived\n   167→    threat. When these diverge, the defender may use force that is\n   168→    proportional to what they believed but disproportional to what\n   169→    actually existed. The Catechism doesn't address this case.\n   170→\n   171→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Either (a) the defender's perception is accurate\n   172→    enough, or (b) proportionality is judged by perceived threat, not\n   173→    actual. The Catechism doesn't specify which. -/\n   174→theorem epistemic_gap_exists\n   175→    (da : DefenseAction)\n   176→    (_h_perceived : proportionalToPerceived da)\n   177→    (h_gap : da.assessment.perceivedThreat > da.assessment.actualThreat) :\n   178→    -- Proportional to perceived threat but NOT to actual threat\n   179→    -- The defender acted \"reasonably\" but used \"too much\" force\n   180→    da.assessment.perceivedThreat > da.assessment.actualThreat :=\n   181→  h_gap\n   182→\n   183→/-!\n   184→## Defense as duty (§2265)\n   185→\n   186→§2265 makes a stronger claim: defense is not just permitted but can be\n   187→a DUTY for those responsible for others. This transforms self-defense\n   188→from a right into an obligation.\n   189→-/\n   190→\n   191→/-- AXIOM 3 (§2265): Defense is a duty for those with responsibility.\n   192→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §2265\n   193→    \"Legitimate defense can be not only a right but a grave duty.\"\n   194→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Having responsibility for others' lives creates\n   195→    a positive obligation to defend them, not just permission. This\n   196→    means a parent who fails to defend their child is morally culpable\n   197→    — even if the defense would require violence. -/\n   198→axiom defense_as_duty :\n   199→  ∀ (defender : Person) (isResponsible : Prop),\n   200→    isResponsible →\n   201→    defender.hasFreeWill = true →\n   202→    -- Defense becomes a duty, not just a right\n   203→    True\n   204→\n   205→/-!\n   206→## The means/ends condition (unstated)\n   207→\n   208→The most important condition the Catechism DOESN'T state: the bad\n   209→effect must not be the MEANS to the good effect. You can't kill the\n   210→aggressor AS a means to save yourself — the killing must be a side\n   211→effect of the defensive action, not the mechanism of defense.\n   212→\n   213→This is subtle: if you push an attacker away and they fall and die,\n   214→the death was a side effect. If you aim at their heart to stop them,\n   215→the death is arguably the means.\n   216→\n   217→The Catechism assumes this condition but doesn't state it.\n   218→-/\n   219→\n   220→/-- The means/ends distinction.\n   221→    The bad effect (death) must not be the mechanism by which the\n   222→    good effect (safety) is achieved. -/\n   223→def badEffectIsSideEffect (dec : DoubleEffectConditions) : Prop :=\n   224→  dec.badNotMeansToGood\n   225→\n   226→/-- Without the means/ends condition, double effect would permit\n   227→    intentional killing as long as it's \"proportionate\" — which\n   228→    would collapse the distinction between legitimate defense and\n   229→    assassination. The unstated condition is load-bearing. -/\n   230→theorem means_condition_prevents_assassination\n   231→    (dec : DoubleEffectConditions)\n   232→    (h_not_means : dec.badNotMeansToGood)\n   233→    (_h_all : dec.isPermissible) :\n   234→    -- The means/ends condition is part of permissibility\n   235→    dec.badNotMeansToGood :=\n   236→  h_not_means\n   237→\n   238→/-!\n   239→## Summary of hidden assumptions\n   240→\n   241→Formalizing §2263-2267 required these assumptions the text doesn't state:\n   242→\n   243→1. **Four conditions, two unstated** — the full double effect doctrine\n   244→   has four conditions; the Catechism states only two (intention and\n   245→   proportionality). The other two (act not intrinsically evil; bad\n   246→   effect not a means to good) are assumed.\n   247→\n   248→2. **Epistemic access to threat level** — proportionality assumes the\n   249→   defender can accurately assess the threat. Real self-defense happens\n   250→   under fear, adrenaline, and imperfect information. The text never\n   251→   addresses the gap between perceived and actual threat.\n   252→\n   253→3. **Defense can be a duty** — for those responsible for others, defense\n   254→   is not just permitted but OBLIGATED. This creates a positive duty to\n   255→   use violence, which is in tension with other Catechism teachings on\n   256→   non-violence.\n   257→\n   258→4. **The means/ends condition** — the most important unstated condition.\n   259→   Without it, the double effect doctrine would permit intentional\n   260→   killing as long as it's proportionate.\n   261→\n   262→The legitimate defense section applies one of the most sophisticated\n   263→principles in Catholic moral theology (double effect) but compresses it\n   264→to the point where two of four conditions are invisible.\n   265→-/\n   266→\n   267→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   268→"}]},"uuid":"a39d0c5f-5289-4c6d-9170-e711b66a5733","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:40:09.108Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a9843526-86cc-47e5-b429-8f6c79ed7c2a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}

{"parentUuid":"cf863599-131d-4517-ae2c-c32aa35afcae","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01LWmRAq8L1wkYD4SR5KBqyZ","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→\n     3→/-!\n     4→# CCC §1987–1993: Justification by Grace\n     5→\n     6→## The Catechism claims\n     7→\n     8→\"The grace of the Holy Spirit has the power to justify us, that is, to\n     9→cleanse us from our sins and to communicate to us 'the righteousness of\n    10→God through faith in Jesus Christ' and through Baptism.\" (§1987)\n    11→\n    12→\"Justification is at the same time the acceptance of God's righteousness\n    13→through faith in Jesus Christ.\" (§1991)\n    14→\n    15→\"Justification has been merited for us by the Passion of Christ.\" (§1992)\n    16→\n    17→\"Justification is conferred in Baptism.\" (§1992)\n    18→\n    19→\"Justification establishes cooperation between God's grace and man's\n    20→freedom.\" (§1993)\n    21→\n    22→## Why this matters\n    23→\n    24→This is the theological fault line of the Reformation. The question:\n    25→how is a sinner made right with God?\n    26→\n    27→- **Catholic (§1987-1993)**: Justification involves BOTH God's grace\n    28→  AND human cooperation. Grace transforms the person internally. Faith,\n    29→  hope, and charity are \"poured into our hearts\" (§1991). The person\n    30→  becomes actually righteous, not just declared so.\n    31→\n    32→- **Protestant (Luther)**: Justification is by faith ALONE (sola fide).\n    33→  Grace does not transform; it COVERS. The sinner is DECLARED righteous\n    34→  but remains a sinner internally (\"simul justus et peccator\").\n    35→\n    36→Formalizing §1987-1993 should expose which axioms make the Catholic\n    37→view distinct from the Protestant one — the axiom set IS the denomination.\n    38→\n    39→## Prediction\n    40→\n    41→I expect this to **require stronger premises AND reveal hidden structure**.\n    42→The Catholic view requires axioms about how grace transforms (not just\n    43→covers), how human cooperation works without earning salvation, and how\n    44→Baptism is the instrument of justification.\n    45→\n    46→## Findings\n    47→\n    48→- **Prediction vs. reality**: Confirmed both. The Catholic position\n    49→  requires: (1) grace is TRANSFORMATIVE, not just forensic — it actually\n    50→  changes the person, (2) human cooperation is enabled BY grace but is\n    51→  genuinely free, (3) Baptism is causally connected to justification\n    52→  (a sacramental axiom), (4) justification is a PROCESS, not a single\n    53→  event, (5) the cooperation axiom creates a tension with §2001 (grace\n    54→  bootstrapping) — the same issue reappears here. The Protestant\n    55→  alternative is obtained by DROPPING axioms 1, 2, and 3, showing that\n    56→  the denominational difference is precisely a difference in axiom sets.\n    57→- **Catholic reading axioms used**: [Scripture] Rom 3:22, Rom 6:3-4;\n    58→  [Tradition] Council of Trent, Session 6 (1547); [Definition] CCC\n    59→  §1987-1993\n    60→- **Surprise level**: Significant — the most surprising finding is that\n    61→  the Catholic-Protestant disagreement can be formalized as a SUBSET\n    62→  RELATION between axiom sets. The Protestant position is (approximately)\n    63→  the Catholic position MINUS the transformation and cooperation axioms.\n    64→  The denominational difference is axiom-set difference.\n    65→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — the axiom-set-as-denomination finding is the\n    66→  most important structural result in the project.\n    67→-/\n    68→\n    69→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology\n    70→\n    71→open Catlib\n    72→\n    73→/-!\n    74→## Models of justification\n    75→\n    76→The Catholic and Protestant views differ in specific, formalizable ways.\n    77→-/\n    78→\n    79→/-- How justification relates to the person's actual state.\n    80→    This is the core disagreement. -/\n    81→inductive JustificationType where\n    82→  /-- Transformative: grace actually changes the person internally.\n    83→      They become genuinely righteous. (Catholic) -/\n    84→  | transformative\n    85→  /-- Forensic: grace declares the person righteous without changing\n    86→      them internally. They remain sinners, covered by grace.\n    87→      (Protestant / Lutheran) -/\n    88→  | forensic\n    89→\n    90→/-- How human beings participate in justification. -/\n    91→inductive HumanRole where\n    92→  /-- Cooperation: the person cooperates with grace through free acts\n    93→      of faith, hope, and charity. (Catholic) -/\n    94→  | cooperation\n    95→  /-- Reception only: the person receives grace through faith alone;\n    96→      no cooperative acts contribute to justification. (Protestant) -/\n    97→  | receptionOnly\n    98→\n    99→/-- The instrument through which justification is conferred. -/\n   100→inductive JustificationInstrument where\n   101→  /-- Baptism — a sacramental act. (Catholic) -/\n   102→  | baptism\n   103→  /-- Faith alone — no sacramental requirement. (Protestant) -/\n   104→  | faithAlone\n   105→\n   106→/-- A model of justification — the set of axioms one accepts. -/\n   107→structure JustificationModel where\n   108→  justType : JustificationType\n   109→  humanRole : HumanRole\n   110→  instrument : JustificationInstrument\n   111→  /-- Is justification a single event or a process? -/\n   112→  isProcess : Bool\n   113→\n   114→/-- The Catholic model of justification (from §1987-1993). -/\n   115→def catholicModel : JustificationModel :=\n   116→  { justType := JustificationType.transformative\n   117→    humanRole := HumanRole.cooperation\n   118→    instrument := JustificationInstrument.baptism\n   119→    isProcess := true }\n   120→\n   121→/-- The (simplified) Protestant model.\n   122→    NOTE: Protestant theology is diverse. This represents the\n   123→    Lutheran \"sola fide\" position, not all Protestants. -/\n   124→def protestantModel : JustificationModel :=\n   125→  { justType := JustificationType.forensic\n   126→    humanRole := HumanRole.receptionOnly\n   127→    instrument := JustificationInstrument.faithAlone\n   128→    isProcess := false }\n   129→\n   130→/-- THE KEY FINDING: The denominational difference is an axiom-set\n   131→    difference. The Catholic and Protestant models differ on exactly\n   132→    three axes: transformation vs. forensic, cooperation vs. reception,\n   133→    sacrament vs. faith alone. -/\n   134→theorem models_differ_on_three_axes :\n   135→    catholicModel.justType ≠ protestantModel.justType ∧\n   136→    catholicModel.humanRole ≠ protestantModel.humanRole ∧\n   137→    catholicModel.instrument ≠ protestantModel.instrument := by\n   138→  refine ⟨?_, ?_, ?_⟩ <;> intro h <;>\n   139→    simp [catholicModel, protestantModel] at h\n   140→\n   141→/-!\n   142→## The Catholic axioms\n   143→\n   144→Each of these is a specific commitment the Catechism makes. Dropping\n   145→any one moves you closer to a different denomination's position.\n   146→-/\n   147→\n   148→/-- AXIOM 1 (§1990-1991): Grace is transformative.\n   149→    Provenance: [Tradition] Council of Trent, Session 6, Canon 11\n   150→    \"Justification... purifies his heart of sin.\" (§1990)\n   151→    \"Faith, hope, and charity are poured into our hearts.\" (§1991)\n   152→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Grace changes the person's actual state —\n   153→    they become genuinely righteous, not just declared so. This is\n   154→    the central Catholic claim that Luther rejected. -/\n   155→axiom grace_is_transformative :\n   156→  ∀ (p : Person),\n   157→    p.hasFreeWill = true →\n   158→    -- After justification, the person is actually (not just\n   159→    -- declared) righteous\n   160→    True\n   161→\n   162→/-- AXIOM 2 (§1993): Human cooperation with grace.\n   163→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1993; [Tradition] Trent Session 6\n   164→    \"Justification establishes cooperation between God's grace and\n   165→    man's freedom.\"\n   166→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: The human person genuinely cooperates in their\n   167→    justification. This cooperation is enabled by grace (connecting\n   168→    to §2001 — the grace bootstrapping) but is genuinely free.\n   169→    Luther's objection: if salvation depends on human cooperation, it's\n   170→    not purely a gift. The Catholic answer: the cooperation itself is\n   171→    a gift of grace. But this is exactly the §2001 bootstrapping\n   172→    problem again. -/\n   173→axiom human_cooperation_in_justification :\n   174→  ∀ (p : Person),\n   175→    p.hasFreeWill = true →\n   176→    -- The person cooperates through free acts of faith/hope/charity\n   177→    -- (this cooperation is itself enabled by grace — see §2001)\n   178→    True\n   179→\n   180→/-- AXIOM 3 (§1992): Baptism is the instrument of justification.\n   181→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1992\n   182→    \"Justification is conferred in Baptism, the sacrament of faith.\"\n   183→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: A sacramental act (performed by the Church)\n   184→    is the ordinary means of justification. This connects soteriology\n   185→    to ecclesiology — you (normally) need the Church to be justified.\n   186→    This is why \"outside the Church there is no salvation\" (with\n   187→    nuances the Catechism addresses elsewhere). -/\n   188→axiom baptism_confers_justification :\n   189→  -- Baptism is the sacramental instrument of justification\n   190→  -- (not faith alone, not works, but a Church-administered rite)\n   191→  True\n   192→\n   193→/-- AXIOM 4 (§1989-1990): Justification is a process.\n   194→    Provenance: [Definition] CCC §1989-1990\n   195→    \"The first work of grace is conversion\" (§1989) followed by\n   196→    purification, reconciliation, freedom, and healing (§1990).\n   197→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: Justification unfolds over time — it's not\n   198→    a single moment of being declared righteous. This is why\n   199→    Catholics talk about \"being justified\" as an ongoing state,\n   200→    while Protestants talk about \"the moment of justification.\" -/\n   201→axiom justification_is_process :\n   202→  -- Justification involves multiple stages:\n   203→  -- conversion → forgiveness → purification → healing\n   204→  -- (not a single event but an unfolding transformation)\n   205→  True\n   206→\n   207→/-!\n   208→## The axiom-set-as-denomination result\n   209→\n   210→The most important structural finding: the difference between Catholic\n   211→and Protestant theology of justification is precisely a difference in\n   212→which axioms you accept. The Protestant position is obtained by:\n   213→\n   214→1. REPLACING transformative with forensic (Axiom 1)\n   215→2. DROPPING human cooperation (Axiom 2)\n   216→3. REPLACING baptism with faith alone (Axiom 3)\n   217→4. REPLACING process with event (Axiom 4)\n   218→\n   219→This is exactly what the PROJECT.md predicted: \"The axiom set IS the\n   220→denomination.\" The Catechism's axioms about justification are the\n   221→Catholic denomination. Change the axioms, change the denomination.\n   222→-/\n   223→\n   224→/-- The Catholic model requires all four axioms. -/\n   225→theorem catholic_requires_all_four :\n   226→    catholicModel.justType = JustificationType.transformative ∧\n   227→    catholicModel.humanRole = HumanRole.cooperation ∧\n   228→    catholicModel.instrument = JustificationInstrument.baptism ∧\n   229→    catholicModel.isProcess = true := by\n   230→  simp [catholicModel]\n   231→\n   232→/-- The Protestant model drops/replaces all four. -/\n   233→theorem protestant_drops_all_four :\n   234→    protestantModel.justType = JustificationType.forensic ∧\n   235→    protestantModel.humanRole = HumanRole.receptionOnly ∧\n   236→    protestantModel.instrument = JustificationInstrument.faithAlone ∧\n   237→    protestantModel.isProcess = false := by\n   238→  simp [protestantModel]\n   239→\n   240→/-!\n   241→## Summary of hidden assumptions\n   242→\n   243→Formalizing §1987-1993 required these assumptions the text doesn't state:\n   244→\n   245→1. **Grace is transformative** — it actually changes the person, not\n   246→   just their legal status before God. This is the central claim that\n   247→   Luther rejected.\n   248→\n   249→2. **Human cooperation is real but grace-enabled** — connecting back to\n   250→   the §2001 bootstrapping problem. Cooperation is a gift, but it's\n   251→   still genuine cooperation.\n   252→\n   253→3. **Baptism is causally connected to justification** — a sacramental\n   254→   axiom that ties soteriology to ecclesiology.\n   255→\n   256→4. **Justification is a process** — not a single event but an unfolding\n   257→   transformation through multiple stages.\n   258→\n   259→5. **THE AXIOM-SET-AS-DENOMINATION FINDING**: The Catholic-Protestant\n   260→   disagreement on justification can be formalized as a difference in\n   261→   axiom sets. Each denomination's position is a specific set of axioms.\n   262→   Change the axioms, change the denomination. This confirms the\n   263→   project's central thesis: the axiom set IS the theological tradition.\n   264→-/\n   265→\n   266→end Catlib.MoralTheology\n   267→"}]},"uuid":"65cf1f4a-7840-4170-87b6-29728bbf7057","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:40:15.700Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cf863599-131d-4517-ae2c-c32aa35afcae","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}

{"parentUuid":"e23ef50c-1719-4111-9f31-e418a7b1516a","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"90bfc06d-2f27-4c98-843f-d48b7935dcd3","agentId":"a507233507e5567eb","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_016e9yngn1gEgy4kBbcJMnhq","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→import Catlib.Foundations\n     2→import Catlib.MoralTheology.SourcesOfMorality\n     3→import Catlib.MoralTheology.LegitimateDefense\n     4→\n     5→/-!\n     6→# CCC §2366-2372: Conjugal Ethics\n     7→\n     8→## The Catechism claims\n     9→\n    10→\"Each and every marital act must of necessity retain its intrinsic\n    11→relationship to the procreation of human life.\" (§2366, citing\n    12→Humanae Vitae §11)\n    13→\n    14→\"For just reasons, spouses may wish to space the births of their\n    15→children. It is their duty to make certain that their desire is not\n    16→motivated by selfishness.\" (§2368)\n    17→\n    18→\"Periodic continence, that is, the methods of birth regulation based on\n    19→self-observation and the use of infertile periods, is in conformity with\n    20→the objective criteria of morality... Every action which... proposes,\n    21→whether as an end or as a means, to render procreation impossible is\n    22→intrinsically evil.\" (§2370, citing Humanae Vitae §14, §16)\n    23→\n    24→\"The political authority... has no right to intervene... by encouraging\n    25→the use of methods of birth regulation contrary to the moral law.\"\n    26→(§2372, citing Populorum Progressio §37)\n    27→\n    28→## Prediction\n    29→\n    30→I expect this to **reveal hidden structure** in the inseparability\n    31→principle — the claim that the unitive and procreative meanings of\n    32→the conjugal act cannot be deliberately separated. This principle is\n    33→the lynchpin: it's what distinguishes the Catholic position on\n    34→contraception from the Protestant position (post-1930). Formalizing\n    35→it should expose:\n    36→\n    37→1. The inseparability principle itself is asserted, not derived.\n    38→2. The NFP/contraception distinction depends on the object-independence\n    39→   axiom from SourcesOfMorality.lean.\n    40→3. \"Just reasons\" for spacing is left deliberately undefined.\n    41→\n    42→## Findings\n    43→\n    44→- **The inseparability principle is an axiom, not a theorem.** The\n    45→  Catechism cites Humanae Vitae, which cites natural law, but never\n    46→  provides a formal derivation. It is a foundational assertion about\n    47→  the nature of the conjugal act.\n    48→- **The NFP/contraception distinction is coherent but depends on\n    49→  the object-independence axiom.** Same outcome (no child), different\n    50→  moral object — this only works if you accept that the moral object\n    51→  can be evaluated independently of consequences.\n    52→- **\"Just reasons\" is a free parameter.** The Catechism provides\n    53→  examples but no decision procedure.\n    54→- **Denominational split**: Pre-1930, all Christians held the\n    55→  inseparability principle. The Anglican Lambeth Conference of 1930\n    56→  (Resolution 15) dropped it. Every other Protestant denomination\n    57→  followed within decades. The Catholic Church reaffirmed it in\n    58→  Casti Connubii (1930) and Humanae Vitae (1968).\n    59→- **Assessment**: Tier 3 — the inseparability principle is the key\n    60→  hidden axiom; the NFP/contraception distinction is its most\n    61→  practically consequential application.\n    62→-/\n    63→\n    64→set_option autoImplicit false\n    65→\n    66→namespace Catlib.MoralTheology.ConjugalEthics\n    67→\n    68→open Catlib\n    69→open Catlib.MoralTheology\n    70→\n    71→/-!\n    72→## The Two Meanings of the Conjugal Act\n    73→\n    74→The Catechism (following Humanae Vitae §12) holds that every conjugal\n    75→act has two inherent meanings — unitive and procreative — and that\n    76→these are inseparable by the design of God.\n    77→-/\n    78→\n    79→/-- The two inherent meanings of the conjugal act (HV §12).\n    80→    The unitive meaning: the act expresses and deepens spousal love.\n    81→    The procreative meaning: the act is ordered toward the generation\n    82→    of new life. -/\n    83→structure ConjugalAct where\n    84→  /-- The spouses performing the act -/\n    85→  spouse1 : Person\n    86→  spouse2 : Person\n    87→  /-- The act retains its unitive meaning (expresses spousal love) -/\n    88→  unitiveIntact : Prop\n    89→  /-- The act retains its procreative meaning (open to life) -/\n    90→  procreativeIntact : Prop\n    91→\n    92→/-- Whether a conjugal act is ordered to both meanings. -/\n    93→def ConjugalAct.bothMeaningsIntact (act : ConjugalAct) : Prop :=\n    94→  act.unitiveIntact ∧ act.procreativeIntact\n    95→\n    96→/-!\n    97→## The Inseparability Principle (Humanae Vitae §12)\n    98→\n    99→This is THE key axiom. Paul VI declared:\n   100→\n   101→\"The Church... teaches that each and every marital act must of necessity\n   102→retain its intrinsic relationship to the procreation of human life.\n   103→This particular doctrine... is based on the inseparable connection,\n   104→established by God, which man on his own initiative may not break,\n   105→between the unitive significance and the procreative significance\n   106→which are both inherent to the marriage act.\"\n   107→\n   108→HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: This principle is asserted, not derived. The\n   109→Catechism says it is \"established by God\" and grounded in natural law,\n   110→but provides no formal argument for WHY the two meanings are\n   111→inseparable. Aquinas didn't argue for it either — in his framework,\n   112→the procreative finality of sex was simply obvious from natural\n   113→teleology. The inseparability principle bundles a teleological claim\n   114→(sex has a natural end: procreation) with a moral claim (deliberately\n   115→frustrating that end is wrong). Whether teleological facts ground\n   116→moral obligations is itself a philosophical question (the is-ought\n   117→gap).\n   118→-/\n   119→\n   120→/-- **AXIOM (HV §12, CCC §2366): THE INSEPARABILITY PRINCIPLE.**\n   121→    The unitive and procreative meanings of the conjugal act may not\n   122→    be deliberately separated.\n   123→\n   124→    Denominational scope:\n   125→    - Pre-1930: ecumenical (all Christians held this)\n   126→    - Post-1930: Catholic only (Anglicans dropped it at Lambeth 1930;\n   127→      other Protestants followed within decades)\n   128→    - The Orthodox position is more nuanced: generally permits\n   129→      contraception within marriage by economia, without a formal\n   130→      doctrinal statement equivalent to Humanae Vitae.\n   131→\n   132→    PROVENANCE: Tradition (Humanae Vitae §12, 1968; reaffirming\n   133→    Casti Connubii §56, 1930; rooted in natural law reasoning\n   134→    from Aquinas ST II-II q.154).\n   135→\n   136→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: This is a foundational assertion, not a\n   137→    derived theorem. The WHY is never formally established. -/\n   138→axiom inseparability_principle :\n   139→  ∀ (act : ConjugalAct),\n   140→    act.unitiveIntact → act.procreativeIntact →\n   141→    act.bothMeaningsIntact\n   142→\n   143→/-- Denominational tag: Catholic (post-1930; pre-1930 ecumenical). -/\n   144→def inseparability_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   145→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.catholic],\n   146→    note := \"Pre-1930: ecumenical. Post-1930: Catholic only (Lambeth 1930 dropped it)\" }\n   147→\n   148→/-!\n   149→## The Separability Principle (the Anglican axiom swap)\n   150→\n   151→For completeness, we model what the 1930 Lambeth Conference actually\n   152→introduced. Resolution 15 effectively replaced the inseparability\n   153→principle with a separability principle: the two meanings CAN be\n   154→separated when there is \"morally sound reason.\"\n   155→\n   156→This is not part of Catholic teaching. We include it to show the\n   157→axiom swap that generated the Protestant position on contraception.\n   158→-/\n   159→\n   160→/-- The separability principle — the axiom Lambeth 1930 introduced.\n   161→    The two meanings of the conjugal act CAN be deliberately separated\n   162→    when there is a morally sound reason.\n   163→\n   164→    NOT accepted in Catholic teaching. Included for ecumenical\n   165→    comparison. -/\n   166→def A1_SEPARABILITY (act : ConjugalAct) (justReason : Prop) : Prop :=\n   167→  justReason → (act.unitiveIntact ∧ ¬act.procreativeIntact → True)\n   168→\n   169→/-- Denominational tag: Protestant post-1930. -/\n   170→def separability_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   171→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.lutheran, Denomination.reformed],\n   172→    note := \"Introduced at Lambeth 1930; adopted by all major Protestant bodies by ~1960\" }\n   173→\n   174→/-!\n   175→## NFP vs. Contraception: The Object Distinction\n   176→\n   177→This is the most contested part of Catholic sexual ethics. The\n   178→Catechism distinguishes:\n   179→- NFP (Natural Family Planning): abstaining during fertile periods\n   180→- Contraception: having sex while rendering procreation impossible\n   181→\n   182→Same OUTCOME (no child conceived), different MORAL OBJECT.\n   183→\n   184→This distinction depends on the object-independence axiom from\n   185→SourcesOfMorality.lean: the moral object of an act can be evaluated\n   186→independently of its consequences.\n   187→-/\n   188→\n   189→/-- The moral object of NFP: choosing not to have sex during fertile\n   190→    periods. The object is abstinence — and abstinence is never evil,\n   191→    because it is the absence of an act, not the performance of a\n   192→    disordered one. -/\n   193→def NFPObject : Prop :=\n   194→  True  -- \"not having sex\" is not an evil act; no positive evil in the object\n   195→\n   196→/-- The moral object of contraception: having sex while deliberately\n   197→    rendering procreation impossible. Under the inseparability\n   198→    principle, this separates what cannot be separated — the unitive\n   199→    act is performed while the procreative meaning is deliberately\n   200→    frustrated. -/\n   201→def ContraceptionObject (act : ConjugalAct) : Prop :=\n   202→  act.unitiveIntact ∧ ¬act.procreativeIntact\n   203→\n   204→/-- Contraception violates the inseparability principle.\n   205→    If the procreative meaning is deliberately frustrated while the\n   206→    unitive meaning is retained, the two meanings have been separated\n   207→    — which the inseparability principle forbids.\n   208→\n   209→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: The \"deliberately\" qualifier is crucial but\n   210→    hard to formalize. The inseparability principle says the meanings\n   211→    cannot be deliberately separated by human initiative. NFP doesn't\n   212→    separate them — it simply abstains during fertile times. The\n   213→    distinction between \"separating\" and \"not acting\" is the entire\n   214→    moral weight of the argument. -/\n   215→theorem contraception_violates_inseparability\n   216→    (act : ConjugalAct)\n   217→    (_h_unitive : act.unitiveIntact)\n   218→    (h_not_procreative : ¬act.procreativeIntact) :\n   219→    ¬act.bothMeaningsIntact := by\n   220→  intro ⟨_, h_proc⟩\n   221→  exact h_not_procreative h_proc\n   222→\n   223→/-- Contraception is intrinsically evil (under inseparability).\n   224→    Since contraception's object is to separate the two meanings,\n   225→    and the inseparability principle says this cannot be done,\n   226→    contraception's object is never good — making it intrinsically\n   227→    evil per the SourcesOfMorality framework. -/\n   228→theorem contraception_intrinsically_evil\n   229→    (act : ConjugalAct)\n   230→    (h_contra : ContraceptionObject act) :\n   231→    IntrinsicallyEvil act.bothMeaningsIntact := by\n   232→  intro ⟨_, h_proc⟩\n   233→  exact h_contra.2 h_proc\n   234→\n   235→/-- Denominational tag: Catholic. -/\n   236→def contraception_evil_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   237→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.catholic],\n   238→    note := \"Pre-1930: ecumenical. CCC §2370, HV §14\" }\n   239→\n   240→/-!\n   241→## NFP Permissibility\n   242→\n   243→NFP is permitted because its moral object is different from\n   244→contraception. The couple abstains during fertile periods — they\n   245→do not perform a conjugal act while frustrating its procreative\n   246→meaning. When they do have conjugal relations (during infertile\n   247→periods), both meanings are intact: the act is unitive and is\n   248→not rendered artificially infertile.\n   249→-/\n   250→\n   251→/-- The object of NFP is not evil. Abstaining from sex is never\n   252→    intrinsically evil — you cannot sin by NOT doing something\n   253→    that is not obligatory. -/\n   254→theorem nfp_object_not_evil : NFPObject := by\n   255→  trivial\n   256→\n   257→/-- A conjugal act during a naturally infertile period retains both\n   258→    meanings. The procreative meaning is not deliberately frustrated\n   259→    — it is simply not actualized due to natural circumstances.\n   260→\n   261→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: There is a morally relevant distinction\n   262→    between \"natural infertility\" and \"artificially induced\n   263→    infertility.\" This distinction is clear under the inseparability\n   264→    principle (one separates; the other doesn't) but is not obvious\n   265→    from a consequentialist perspective (same outcome either way). -/\n   266→axiom nfp_preserves_both_meanings :\n   267→  ∀ (act : ConjugalAct),\n   268→    act.unitiveIntact →\n   269→    act.procreativeIntact →\n   270→    -- The act during an infertile period still has both meanings\n   271→    act.bothMeaningsIntact\n   272→\n   273→/-- NFP is permissible: the moral evaluation can be good.\n   274→    Given that NFP's object is not evil, and the intention is\n   275→    responsible parenthood (a good intention), and circumstances\n   276→    are appropriate (just reasons for spacing), the three sources\n   277→    of morality are satisfied. -/\n   278→theorem nfp_is_permissible\n   279→    (eval : MoralEvaluation)\n   280→    (h_obj : eval.objectIsGood)\n   281→    (h_int : eval.intentionIsGood)\n   282→    (h_circ : eval.circumstancesAppropriate) :\n   283→    eval.isGood := by\n   284→  exact ⟨h_obj, h_int, h_circ⟩\n   285→\n   286→/-- Denominational tag: Catholic. -/\n   287→def nfp_tag : DenominationalTag :=\n   288→  { acceptedBy := [Denomination.catholic],\n   289→    note := \"CCC §2370; HV §16. Protestant view: NFP unnecessary since contraception is permitted\" }\n   290→\n   291→/-!\n   292→## Responsible Parenthood (CCC §2368)\n   293→\n   294→\"For just reasons, spouses may wish to space the births of their\n   295→children.\"\n   296→\n   297→HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: \"Just reasons\" is a free parameter. The Catechism\n   298→gives examples (health, economic hardship, existing children's\n   299→welfare) but provides no formal decision procedure. Who decides\n   300→what counts as \"just\"? The answer is: the couple's informed\n   301→conscience (S9, conscience binds). But this creates a tension:\n   302→conscience is fallible, and what one couple considers \"just\" another\n   303→might not. The Catechism doesn't resolve this tension — it trusts\n   304→the conscience while insisting the conscience must be properly\n   305→formed.\n   306→-/\n   307→\n   308→/-- Just reasons for spacing births.\n   309→    This is deliberately left as an opaque Prop because the\n   310→    Catechism itself does not define it precisely.\n   311→    Examples given: health of the mother, economic hardship,\n   312→    welfare of existing children.\n   313→\n   314→    HIDDEN ASSUMPTION: \"Just reasons\" is undefined. This is a\n   315→    genuine gap in the formalization — and in the Catechism. -/\n   316→opaque JustReasons : Prop\n   317→\n   318→/-- Responsible parenthood: spacing for just reasons using NFP\n   319→    is morally permissible. CCC §2368.\n   320→    Requires: (1) just reasons exist, (2) method used is NFP\n   321→    (not contraception), (3) motivation is not selfish. -/\n   322→structure ResponsibleParenthood where\n   323→  /-- Just reasons for spacing exist -/\n   324→  justReasons : JustReasons\n   325→  /-- The method respects the inseparability principle -/\n   326→  methodRespectsInseparability : Prop\n   327→  /-- The desire is not motivated by selfishness -/\n   328→  notSelfish : Prop\n   329→\n   330→/-- Responsible parenthood is permissible when all conditions hold. -/\n   331→def ResponsibleParenthood.isPermissible (rp : ResponsibleParenthood) : Prop :=\n   332→  rp.methodRespectsInseparability ∧ rp.notSelfish\n   333→\n   334→/-!\n   335→## Medical Double Effect (CCC §2370 note)\n   336→\n   337→Medical treatment that has a contraceptive side effect is permitted\n   338→under the principle of double effect (formalized in\n   339→LegitimateDefense.lean). The classic case: hormonal treatment for\n   340→endometriosis that also suppresses ovulation.\n   341→\n   342→This uses the same DoubleEffectConditions structure from\n   343→LegitimateDefense.lean.\n   344→-/\n   345→\n   346→/-- A medical treatment with a contraceptive side effect. -/\n   347→structure MedicalTreatmentWithSideEffect where\n   348→  /-- The patient -/\n   349→  patient : Person\n   350→  /-- The medical condition being treated -/\n   351→  medicalCondition : Prop\n   352→  /-- The treatment addresses the condition (good effect) -/\n   353→  treatsCondition : Prop\n   354→  /-- The treatment has a contraceptive side effect (bad effect) -/\n   355→  contraceptiveSideEffect : Prop\n   356→  /-- The treatment's PURPOSE is medical, not contraceptive -/\n   357→  intendsMedical : Prop\n   358→  /-- The contraceptive effect is NOT intended -/\n   359→  doesNotIntendContraception : Prop\n   360→\n   361→/-- Medical treatment with contraceptive side effect as a double\n   362→    effect act. Maps to the DoubleEffectAct structure. -/\n   363→def MedicalTreatmentWithSideEffect.toDoubleEffectAct\n   364→    (mt : MedicalTreatmentWithSideEffect) : DoubleEffectAct :=\n   365→  { agent := mt.patient\n   366→  , goodEffect := mt.treatsCondition\n   367→  , badEffect := mt.contraceptiveSideEffect\n   368→  , intendsGood := mt.intendsMedical\n   369→  , doesNotIntendBad := mt.doesNotIntendContraception\n   370→  }\n   371→\n   372→/-- Medical treatment is permissible under double effect when all\n   373→    four conditions are met:\n   374→    1. The act (taking medicine) is not intrinsically evil\n   375→    2. The intention is medical treatment, not contraception\n   376→    3. The contraceptive effect is not the means to the medical benefit\n   377→    4. The medical benefit is proportionate to the side effect\n   378→\n   379→    This is a direct application of the double effect framework\n   380→    from LegitimateDefense.lean. -/\n   381→theorem medical_treatment_permissible\n   382→    (dec : DoubleEffectConditions)\n   383→    (h_not_evil : dec.actNotIntrinsicallyEvil)\n   384→    (h_intended : dec.goodIntended)\n   385→    (h_not_means : dec.badNotMeansToGood)\n   386→    (h_proportionate : dec.proportionate) :\n   387→    dec.isPermissible := by\n   388→  exact ⟨h_not_evil, h_intended, h_not_means, h_proportionate⟩\n   389→\n   390→/-!\n   391→## State Authority and Contraception (CCC §2372)\n   392→\n   393→\"Regulating the population... cannot justify courses of action which\n   394→are in themselves contrary to the moral law.\" The State has no\n   395→authority to impose contraception as population policy.\n   396→-/\n   397→\n   398→/-- The State lacks authority to impose methods contrary to the\n   399→    moral law, including contraception. CCC §2372.\n   400→    This follows from the general principle that state authority\n   401→    is delegated (from Basic.lean) and cannot override natural law. -/\n   402→axiom state_no_authority_to_impose_contraception :\n   403→  ∀ (stateAuth : Authority),\n   404→    stateAuth.source = AuthoritySource.naturalLaw →\n   405→    -- Even natural-law-based authority cannot impose intrinsically\n   406→    -- evil methods — authority does not extend to commanding evil\n   407→    True\n   408→\n   409→/-!\n   410→## Summary of Hidden Assumptions\n   411→\n   412→Formalizing §2366-2372 required these assumptions the text doesn't\n   413→fully justify:\n   414→\n   415→1. **The inseparability principle itself** — WHY can't you separate\n   416→   the unitive and procreative meanings? The Catechism says it's\n   417→   \"established by God\" and grounded in natural law, but provides\n   418→   no derivation. This is the foundational axiom. Pre-1930, all\n   419→   Christians accepted it. Post-1930, only Catholics (and some\n   420→   Orthodox) maintain it.\n   421→\n   422→2. **The act/object distinction between NFP and contraception** —\n   423→   same outcome (no baby), different moral object (abstaining vs.\n   424→   rendering procreation impossible). This distinction is coherent\n   425→   but depends on the object-independence axiom: the moral object\n   426→   of an act can be evaluated independently of its consequences.\n   427→   Consequentialists reject this axiom, which is why they see no\n   428→   moral difference between NFP and contraception.\n   429→\n   430→3. **\"Just reasons\" is undefined** — the Catechism says couples\n   431→   may space births for \"just reasons\" but never defines what\n   432→   counts. This is delegated to the couple's informed conscience\n   433→   (S9), which means the framework trusts human judgment on a\n   434→   question it considers very important.\n   435→\n   436→4. **Natural vs. artificial infertility** — the distinction between\n   437→   \"sex during a naturally infertile period\" (morally fine) and\n   438→   \"sex after artificially inducing infertility\" (intrinsically evil)\n   439→   depends entirely on the inseparability principle. If you drop\n   440→   inseparability, this distinction evaporates — which is exactly\n   441→   what happened in Protestantism after 1930.\n   442→\n   443→5. **The denominational fracture is an axiom swap** — dropping the\n   444→   inseparability principle and adopting A1_SEPARABILITY is the\n   445→   single axiom change that generates the entire Protestant position\n   446→   on contraception. Every other difference (permitting the pill,\n   447→   permitting condoms, etc.) follows from this one swap.\n   448→-/\n   449→\n   450→end Catlib.MoralTheology.ConjugalEthics\n   451→"}]},"uuid":"727f7dac-1897-4d76-a9ac-46b58ba3f603","timestamp":"2026-03-22T07:40:16.143Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e23ef50c-1719-4111-9f31-e418a7b1516a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/home/ngundotra/Documents/catlib","sessionId":"8380db11-61bc-4b9e-bc7e-b8c33a25885e","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"master","slug":"hidden-honking-hellman"}
